This separation between category formation and instance classification maps closely onto the separation the Nāṭyaśāstra's own sāmānya/viśeṣa terminology draws between the general form, established once through direct training, and the particular application, requiring a further, situation-specific act of correct classification and modification each time it occurs.

Lakṣaṇa — defining categorization precisely against neighboring operations

It is worth being precise about what categorization is not, since the term is sometimes used loosely to cover any act of mental grouping whatsoever. Categorization in the technical sense used throughout the cognitive-scientific literature refers specifically to the assignment of a particular instance to one member of an already-established, reusable set of general classes, distinguishing it from at least two neighboring operations: simple discrimination, the ability merely to tell two instances apart without necessarily assigning either to any named general class, and one-off individuation, the recognition of a single unique particular as itself, with no claim to generality across other instances at all. A performer distinguishing two slightly different executions of the same hasta from one another is engaged in discrimination; a performer recognizing a specific viśeṣa application as an instance of a known general category is engaged in categorization proper; and an audience member forming a memory of one specific unrepeated performance moment, without any claim that this moment instantiates a recognized general type, is engaged in individuation. All three operations occur constantly in live performance and reception, but only categorization — the third of the three in isolation would leave nothing transferable to future encounters — is what supports the kind of cumulative, transferable skill the tradition's training methods are built to produce.

Prakriyā — the developmental and comparative evidence for categorization as universal

The claim that categorization is a cognitive universal rather than a specialized skill confined to particular cultures, particular training regimes, or particular species is supported by two independent bodies of evidence documented within cognitive and developmental psychology. Developmental research finds that infants, well before the acquisition of language and well before any formal instruction of any kind, already demonstrate measurable sensitivity to category structure — for instance, showing renewed attention, a documented marker of novelty detection, when a sequence of visually similar items is interrupted by an item from a different category, even when the infant has received no explicit teaching about either category and the categories in question are not named in any language the infant has yet acquired. This finding indicates that some capacity for extracting shared structure across varied instances is operative extremely early in cognitive development, prior to and largely independent of formal instruction, rather than being a skill that must be painstakingly built from nothing by explicit teaching alone.

Comparative research across non-human species provides a second, independent line of evidence: categorization behavior, assessed through tasks in which an animal must respond consistently to novel instances of a trained category while withholding that same response from instances of a contrasting category, has been documented across a wide range of species with very different neural architectures and very different natural ecologies, from pigeons trained on visual discrimination tasks to primates trained on more complex hierarchical category structures. The breadth of this cross-species evidence indicates that some form of categorization capacity is not a specifically human cognitive achievement bound up with language, but a considerably more general computational solution to the problem of extracting reusable structure from variable sensory experience, a solution that human cultural training systems — including the Nāṭyaśāstra's own sāmānya/viśeṣa pedagogy — can subsequently recruit, refine, and deliberately optimize far beyond whatever baseline categorization capacity is present without training.

Udāharaṇa — categorization failure as diagnostic evidence for categorization's normal operation

One of the more informative sources of evidence for how categorization ordinarily functions comes, perhaps counterintuitively, from documented cases in which it fails to function normally. Certain forms of acquired brain injury, most informatively injury localized to specific regions of temporal and frontal cortex, have been documented to selectively impair categorization while leaving other, superficially similar cognitive capacities comparatively intact — a patient may retain the ability to perceive an object's individual visual features accurately, and may retain considerable general knowledge acquired before the injury, while nonetheless showing a specific, measurable difficulty correctly classifying novel instances against previously known categories, or a specific difficulty forming stable new categories from post-injury experience. This double dissociation between intact perception and intact prior knowledge, on the one hand, and impaired categorization specifically, on the other, is exactly the kind of evidence cognitive neuroscience treats as indicating that categorization is a genuinely separable cognitive operation with its own dedicated, damageable neural substrate, rather than being merely an automatic byproduct of perception or memory functioning normally elsewhere in the brain.

Phala — the pedagogical stakes of treating categorization as foundational

If categorization is, as this evidence indicates, a genuine, separable, foundational cognitive operation rather than an automatic byproduct of other capacities, then a training system that deliberately and explicitly cultivates strong sāmānya abstraction — rather than relying on incidental exposure to accumulate it as an unplanned side effect of viśeṣa practice — is training something with independent cognitive reality and independent cognitive value, not merely organizing curriculum content for convenience of presentation. This gives a direct, non-metaphorical justification, external to the tradition's own internal rationale, for the emphasis the Nāṭyaśāstra places on the sāmānya form as a distinct object of training in its own right: the tradition is, on this account, training a genuine and independently identifiable cognitive faculty, and doing so through a method — isolated repetition of an invariant target prior to variation — that independently developed cognitive-scientific research on category learning has separately identified as effective for exactly this purpose, using populations, materials, and methods with no historical connection whatsoever to the śāstric tradition itself.

03

From Classical to Prototype Theories of Categories

For much of the twentieth century, the dominant assumption in both philosophy and early cognitive psychology was what is now called the classical theory of categories: the view, traceable to Aristotelian logic, that every category is defined by a fixed list of necessary and sufficient features, such that any object either possesses all the required features and is unambiguously a member of the category, or lacks at least one required feature and is unambiguously excluded from it, with no intermediate or graded cases permitted by the logic of the definition itself.

This classical assumption was substantially overturned by a body of experimental work conducted principally in the nineteen-seventies, which demonstrated that ordinary human categories, as they actually function in memory and judgment, very rarely behave as the classical theory predicts. Subjects asked to rate how good an example a given item is of a familiar category consistently produce graded ratings rather than uniform yes-or-no judgments — a robin is reliably judged a more typical bird than a penguin, even though both are, by any classical checklist definition of "bird," equally members of the category — and subjects are measurably faster and more accurate at verifying category membership for highly typical instances than for atypical but still genuine instances, an effect that a strict all-or-nothing classical category structure would not predict at all, since classical membership does not admit of degree.

These findings led to prototype theory, which holds that categories are represented in memory not as a checklist of defining features but as a prototype: an abstracted representation of the most typical, central member of the category, built up from the statistical regularities across many encountered instances, with judgments about new or borderline instances made by assessing their similarity to this central prototype rather than by checking them against a fixed feature list. A category, on this account, has a graded internal structure — some members close to the prototype and judged highly typical, others more peripheral and judged less typical — rather than a sharp, uniform boundary separating members from non-members. Subsequent research has refined and partially qualified prototype theory in various ways, including proposals that categories are represented through stored specific exemplars rather than, or in addition to, a single abstracted prototype; but the central empirical finding that motivated the shift away from the classical theory — that category membership and typicality are graded rather than uniform — has remained robust across this subsequent refinement.

Lakṣaṇa — the specific empirical signatures that separate the two theories

Two experimental signatures in particular are worth stating precisely. The first is the typicality-rating signature: when subjects are asked to rate exemplars of a category on a numerical scale of how good or representative an example each is, ratings distribute continuously rather than clustering at only the two extremes a strictly classical, checklist-based category would predict. The second is the reaction-time signature: when subjects are asked to verify, as quickly as possible, whether a given item belongs to a given category, response latencies are shorter and error rates lower for items independently rated as highly typical than for items independently rated as less typical but still genuine members, with this latency difference persisting even when the classical, checklist-based membership status of the two items is identical. Both signatures indicate that whatever representation subjects are actually consulting when they make these judgments has graded internal structure, rather than the flat, undifferentiated structure a classical checklist definition would produce.

Prakriyā — how the classical theory's specific predictions were tested and failed

The classical theory's decisive vulnerability, from an experimental standpoint, was that it made a strong and directly falsifiable prediction: if category membership is genuinely all-or-nothing, determined by a fixed feature checklist, then every member of a category ought to be equally good an example of it, differing at most in how quickly a given feature can be visually confirmed, but not in any more fundamental sense of "how much" a given member belongs to the category. Researchers exploited exactly this prediction by constructing category-membership judgment tasks in which classical membership was held constant — every tested item was, by any reasonable checklist definition, an equally genuine member of the category — while typicality was allowed to vary freely, as independently established by a separate group of raters. Under the classical theory's own prediction, response time and accuracy on these tasks should not have varied with typicality at all, since typicality was defined as orthogonal to strict membership; the finding that response time and accuracy did vary systematically and considerably with typicality, despite membership itself being held constant, is what constituted the direct empirical falsification of the classical theory's core claim, rather than merely a finding that sat awkwardly alongside it.

Udāharaṇa — the furniture and bird category studies as worked cases

The category "furniture" provides one of the more extensively documented worked cases in this literature. Items such as chair and sofa are consistently rated, across independent groups of raters, as highly typical furniture, while items such as lamp or telephone, though frequently included by subjects within a broadly construed "furniture" category in some studies and excluded in others, are rated considerably less typical when included at all, and items such as stove or fan occupy a genuinely graded, contested middle ground on which raters themselves disagree. This graded disagreement at the category's periphery, rather than a sharp and universally agreed boundary, is itself a further piece of evidence against the classical theory, since a genuine checklist-based definition, correctly applied, ought to produce consistent classification of borderline cases across raters rather than the documented rater-to-rater disagreement actually observed. The bird category provides the second classic worked case, with the specific documented finding that robin, sparrow, and similar small perching birds are consistently rated more typical than ostrich, penguin, or chicken, despite all being, by any ordinary biological checklist, equally genuine members of the category "bird."

Phala — why the shift from classical to prototype theory matters beyond psychology itself

The shift from classical to prototype theory matters here for a specific reason beyond its intrinsic interest within cognitive psychology: it supplies an independently validated, empirically grounded theoretical vocabulary — prototype, typicality gradient, graded category membership — that turns out to describe, with considerable precision, a structural feature the Nāṭyaśāstra's sāmānya/viśeṣa distinction was already implicitly built around many centuries before this vocabulary existed in its present form. On grounds entirely internal to cognitive psychology's own experimental history, graded prototype structure is not a special or unusual feature confined to invented laboratory categories, but a documented, general property of how human categorization actually operates across the ordinary categories of everyday cognition — which is what makes it a non-arbitrary point of comparison for a deliberately constructed technical vocabulary such as the hasta inventory, rather than a superficial analogy chosen for rhetorical convenience.

04

Prototype Structure in the Sāmānya Form

The sāmānya form of a hasta or a guṇa functions, on inspection, precisely as a prototype functions in the technical sense established by prototype theory: it is the single, centrally taught version of the category, referenced whenever the category is named in isolation, and it is the version every documented viśeṣa application is understood as a graded modification of, rather than each viśeṣa application constituting an independent, unrelated category with its own separate name and its own separate training requirement.

This has a direct, measurable consequence for the cognitive economy of training. If each viśeṣa application of paṭāka were, in fact, stored and retrieved as an entirely separate category unconnected to any shared prototype, a performer would need to independently memorize as many distinct motor and perceptual categories as the tradition documents specific applications for — a number considerably larger, across the full hasta inventory, than the number of base hand-shapes themselves. Because the viśeṣa applications are instead organized around a shared sāmānya prototype, the actual memory burden is very much smaller: one prototype, plus a comparatively short list of modification principles — angle, speed, accompanying facial cue, combination with a second hasta — that recombine to generate the full documented range of specific meanings. This is the same graded, prototype-centered economy prototype theory documents for everyday categories such as "bird" or "chair," applied here to a domain-specific, deliberately constructed technical vocabulary rather than to a category acquired informally from unstructured exposure.

A further parallel concerns typicality effects. Just as subjects are faster and more accurate judging a robin than a penguin as a bird, a trained performer and a trained audience member are, on the available anecdotal and pedagogical evidence surrounding hasta recognition, faster and more confident recognizing a hasta held in its canonical sāmānya position and orientation than recognizing the same hasta in a more peripheral, heavily modified viśeṣa application, particularly one that combines several modification principles simultaneously. This typicality gradient is precisely what a prototype-structured category predicts and a classical, all-or-nothing category structure does not, and it gives a further reason, beyond simple economy of memorization, for training to begin with the canonical sāmānya form: the canonical form is not merely easier to learn first, but is also, once learned, the fastest and most reliably recognized point of reference against which every more peripheral variation is subsequently read by an audience.

Lakṣaṇa — distinguishing an engineered prototype from an emergent one

A distinction worth making explicit, and one the tradition's own account does not itself need to make since it is concerned with practice rather than with categorization theory, is between an emergent prototype and an engineered one. The prototypes prototype theory was originally developed to describe — "bird," "furniture," "fruit" — are emergent: no single authority designed the robin to be the most typical bird, and the graded structure of the category arose unplanned from the accumulated statistical regularities of ordinary experience across a whole population of language users. The sāmānya form of a hasta, by contrast, is an engineered prototype: it was, at some point in the tradition's textual and pedagogical history, deliberately fixed, named, and prescribed as the canonical reference form, rather than emerging unplanned from unstructured population-level exposure. This is not a claim that the hasta system spontaneously acquired prototype structure the way "bird" did, but the stronger and more specific claim that a deliberately engineered technical vocabulary, designed centuries before prototype theory existed, independently arrived at the same graded, single-reference-point structure that prototype theory subsequently discovered to be characteristic of naturally emergent categories, a structure that is, if anything, more striking for being unplanned on one side of the comparison and deliberately engineered on the other.

Prakriyā — how canonical-form training builds a psychologically real prototype

The mechanism by which sustained sāmānya drilling is expected to produce a genuine, psychologically real prototype representation, rather than merely a well-rehearsed single memorized instance indistinguishable in kind from any other memorized instance, rests on a documented feature of how prototype abstraction is thought to occur even in the emergent, unplanned case: repeated exposure to multiple instances of a category, even where those instances vary somewhat from occasion to occasion, is what drives the formation of an abstracted central-tendency representation, since the abstraction process depends on statistical averaging across instances rather than on the mere repetition of one single unvarying instance. Sāmānya training, properly conducted, is not the repetition of one perfectly identical motor sequence on every trial — no two physical repetitions of any motor sequence are ever perfectly identical, however well-drilled — but the repetition of the same intended target form across many individually slightly-varying physical executions, which is exactly the condition under which prototype-abstraction mechanisms are documented to operate most effectively, extracting the stable, intended central tendency from across the small execution-to-execution variability every individual repetition actually contains.

Udāharaṇa — a comparative case from a non-Indian gestural tradition

A useful comparative check on this account, offered here as a structural-synthetic proposal rather than as a directly documented claim within either literature, comes from considering the general design of other deliberately engineered gestural sign systems, such as the manual alphabets and formally standardized sign vocabularies developed for sign languages, which also document a canonical, dictionary-citation form for each sign — comparable in function to a hasta's sāmānya form — alongside a substantial range of context-dependent modifications made to that citation form in fluent, connected signing, modifications governed by principles of location, handshape, and movement that a fluent signer applies systematically rather than idiosyncratically. Sign-language linguistics documents this citation-versus-connected-use distinction using vocabulary considerably closer to the Nāṭyaśāstra's own sāmānya/viśeṣa pair than to prototype theory's original bird-and-furniture examples, since both the hasta system and formal sign-language lexicons are engineered rather than emergent vocabularies; the parallel is offered here as suggestive of a general design principle likely to recur across deliberately constructed gestural sign systems generally, rather than as an established historical or comparative-linguistic claim connecting the two traditions directly.

Phala — the training implication of prototype-centered organization

If the sāmānya form functions as a genuine cognitive prototype rather than merely as a conventional label for "the first thing taught," the training implication is specific and falls out directly from what is independently known about how prototype-based categories are best acquired: a category organized around a single stable central prototype is most efficiently taught by ensuring that prototype is itself well-formed and stable in the learner's memory before peripheral, less typical variants are introduced, since introducing highly variable peripheral instances before the central tendency has stabilized measurably disrupts prototype formation in the general categorization literature. This gives independent cognitive-scientific grounding, beyond the tradition's own accumulated pedagogical experience, for treating premature viśeṣa exposure as a genuine training risk rather than merely a matter of traditional preference.

05

The Neural Architecture of Category Abstraction

Neuroscientific investigation of category learning, using both functional neuroimaging in humans and single-cell electrophysiological recording in non-human primates trained on categorization tasks, has converged on a broadly consistent picture of which brain structures support the formation and use of abstracted, prototype-like category representations. The prefrontal cortex, and in particular its more anterior and lateral regions, is consistently implicated in representing category information at a relatively abstracted level, encoding the category membership of a stimulus in a form that generalizes across considerable variation in the stimulus's specific perceptual features — precisely the kind of representation prototype theory posits at the psychological level.

Working alongside prefrontal cortex, structures in the medial temporal lobe, most centrally the hippocampus, are implicated specifically in the formation of new categorical schemas from repeated exposure to varied instances, and in the consolidation of these newly formed category representations into more stable long-term memory over time and, in many studied cases, over sleep. This gives a two-stage picture of category learning at the neural level: an initial, hippocampally supported process of extracting statistical regularities from multiple varied exposures to instances of a to-be-learned category, followed by a more gradual process, implicating prefrontal cortex more heavily as learning proceeds, of representing the resulting abstracted category in a stable, generalizable form that can be applied efficiently to new instances without each new instance requiring the full, effortful comparison process the earliest exposures required.

This neural picture is directly relevant to how sāmānya training is structured. Extended, repeated drilling of a sāmānya form in relative isolation, before viśeṣa variation is introduced, is training exactly the kind of process this neural evidence associates with stable, generalizable category formation: repeated exposure to a single, consistent target form, allowing hippocampal schema-extraction and prefrontal abstraction to jointly establish a stable, generalized representation of that form before the more varied, instance-specific viśeṣa applications are introduced and begin laying down their own more particular associative memory traces on top of an already-stabilized general prototype. Introducing viśeṣa variation too early in training, before this general representation has stabilized, risks exactly the outcome the neural evidence would predict for premature exposure to high variability: a less clearly abstracted, less reliably generalizable underlying category representation, with correspondingly less reliable recognition and production of the sāmānya form itself once viśeṣa variation is layered on top of it.

Lakṣaṇa — specifying what "abstraction" means at the level of single neurons

At the level of single-cell recording, category abstraction has a specific, documented electrophysiological signature worth stating precisely rather than left as a loose metaphor: a neuron participating in an abstracted category representation is one whose firing rate reliably distinguishes category membership — responding strongly to any instance of the trained category and weakly to any instance of a contrasting category — while showing comparatively little sensitivity to the specific perceptual features that happen to distinguish one within-category instance from another. This is a stronger and more specific claim than merely finding neurons that respond to the trained stimuli at all; it is a claim about a specific pattern of generalization across within-category variation coupled with a specific pattern of discrimination across the category boundary, and it is exactly this dual pattern — high within-category generalization, sharp between-category discrimination — that recorded prefrontal neurons in primate categorization studies have been documented to display, distinguishing genuine abstracted category coding from the simpler case of neurons that merely respond to specific trained stimuli without any generalization to novel category-consistent instances at all.

Prakriyā — the documented two-stage timeline of category learning

The two-stage hippocampal-then-prefrontal timeline has a documented approximate temporal structure worth making explicit. In the earliest stage of learning a new category from varied exposure, hippocampal involvement is comparatively pronounced, consistent with the hippocampus's broader documented role in rapidly forming new associative memories from limited exposure, before those memories have had the opportunity to be gradually consolidated into more distributed, cortically stored long-term representations. As training on a given category continues and correct categorization performance stabilizes, the relative involvement of prefrontal regions increases while hippocampal involvement for that specific, now-well-learned category comparatively recedes, a shift documented in neuroimaging studies as training on a given category set proceeds across multiple sessions, and consistent with the broader neuroscientific picture of memory consolidation in which newly formed memories depend more heavily on hippocampal structures early on and become progressively less hippocampally dependent, and more dependent on distributed cortical representation, as consolidation proceeds over time and, the evidence indicates, over subsequent sleep.

Udāharaṇa — a worked training-design case

Applied concretely to sāmānya training for a single hasta, this two-stage picture suggests a specific worked prediction, offered here explicitly as a structural-synthetic proposal extrapolated from general categorization neuroscience rather than as a claim directly tested on hasta training itself: a learner in the first several sessions of drilling a new sāmānya form is, on this account, primarily engaged in the earlier, more hippocampally-mediated stage of extracting a stable target representation from the small execution-to-execution variability of repeated practice, a stage plausibly more effortful, more consciously monitored, and more error-prone than the more fluent, automatic-feeling production the same form eventually achieves once, on this account, prefrontal abstraction has taken over the bulk of the representational work. If viśeṣa variation is introduced during this still-hippocampally-dependent early window — before the account predicts the more stable, generalizable prefrontal-level representation has had the opportunity to consolidate — the new, more variable viśeṣa-related learning would be expected, on general principles of interference in memory consolidation, to compete with and potentially disrupt the still-fragile sāmānya consolidation process, providing a specific, mechanistically motivated reason to prefer a training pause of a documented consolidation-relevant duration, plausibly spanning at least one intervening sleep period, between initial sāmānya stabilization and the introduction of viśeṣa variation.

Phala — an explicit note on the limits of this evidence

The hippocampal-prefrontal two-stage account is well documented for laboratory categorization tasks, typically involving comparatively simple visual or auditory stimulus categories learned over a period of days to weeks under controlled experimental conditions; it has not, to date, been directly tested using hasta training or any comparably complex, culturally embedded, multi-year gestural skill as the object of study. Its application to hasta pedagogy is a reasoned extrapolation from a well-established general mechanism to a specific untested domain sharing relevant structural features with the domains in which the mechanism has been established, not a report of findings specific to Nāṭyaśāstra training, which do not at present exist in the peer-reviewed neuroscientific literature.

06

Viśeṣa as Context-Dependent Specialization

If sāmānya corresponds to an abstracted, prototype-like category representation, viśeṣa corresponds to the further cognitive operation of specializing that general representation for a specific context — a distinct process, requiring its own separate account, since knowing a general category does not by itself determine which of several possible specific instances of that category is correct or intended in a given situation. A performer who has mastered paṭāka's sāmānya form still faces, on any given occasion of use, the further task of selecting and executing the correct viśeṣa modification for the specific dramatic content of that moment, and an audience member watching faces the parallel further task of correctly inferring, from the specific modification observed, which of the hasta's several documented meanings is intended on this particular occasion.

This context-dependent specialization is closely related to what cognitive psychology studies under the heading of context-dependent memory and context-dependent retrieval: the well-documented finding that the same stored general knowledge is accessed and applied differently depending on the surrounding context present at the moment of retrieval, with contextual cues serving to narrow a broad category down to the specific instance appropriate to the current situation. A word with several possible meanings, encountered in a sentence that supplies strong contextual constraint, is read and understood in its contextually appropriate sense rapidly and with little conscious deliberation, whereas the same word encountered with minimal surrounding context produces measurably slower and less certain interpretation, precisely because the specialization process that narrows the general category down to the intended particular instance has less contextual material available to work with.

Viśeṣa specialization in performance operates on the same underlying principle. The available context at the moment a hasta, guṇa, or bhāva is deployed — the preceding narrative content, the established dramatic register, the accompanying facial expression and vocal delivery occurring simultaneously in other channels — functions exactly as contextual cues function in verbal disambiguation, narrowing the broad sāmānya category down to one specific intended viśeṣa reading among several the base form could in principle support. This is why the same sāmānya hasta, produced with minimal surrounding context, is measurably more ambiguous to an audience than the identical hasta produced within a richly established dramatic situation: the underlying category has not changed, but the contextual material available to specialize it down to one specific reading has changed considerably, and it is this contextual specialization process, rather than any change in the hasta's own physical execution, that principally determines how confidently and how quickly an audience arrives at the intended particular meaning.

Lakṣaṇa — specialization as selection among an already-known set, not creation of new meaning

A precise statement of what viśeṣa specialization does and does not involve is useful here, since the term "specialization" could otherwise be misread as implying that new meaning is somehow generated fresh at the moment of contextual narrowing. On the account developed in this section, viśeṣa specialization is a selection operation among an already-existing, previously learned set of documented specific applications associated with a given sāmānya base, rather than a generative operation producing genuinely novel meaning on the spot; the lexical-ambiguity-resolution parallel drawn in the lede is exact on precisely this point, since a reader disambiguating a genuinely ambiguous word is selecting among the word's pre-existing stored senses using contextual cues, not inventing a new sense of the word in the moment. This has a direct consequence for what viśeṣa training must actually accomplish: it is not sufficient for a performer merely to have absorbed the sāmānya form and to possess good general contextual judgment, since context alone cannot generate a meaning the performer has never specifically learned; the full documented set of viśeṣa applications for a given sāmānya form must itself have been separately learned, so that context has a pre-existing menu of specific meanings to select among rather than nothing to select from at all.

Prakriyā — the cross-modal timing of contextual narrowing

Verbal lexical-disambiguation research documents a specific and relevant temporal profile for how contextual narrowing actually unfolds moment to moment, worth transferring carefully to the performance case: multiple possible senses of an ambiguous word are documented, under controlled experimental conditions, to be briefly and simultaneously activated in the earliest moments after the word is encountered, with contextually inappropriate senses then suppressed and the contextually appropriate sense selected within a documented short interval on the order of a few hundred milliseconds, rather than the inappropriate senses simply never being activated at all. Applied to viśeṣa reception, this timing profile predicts, as a structural-synthetic extrapolation rather than a directly tested claim, that an audience member observing a hasta briefly entertains, in the earliest moment of visual processing, more than one of the hasta's documented possible meanings, with surrounding dramatic context then rapidly suppressing the contextually inappropriate readings and settling on the single intended viśeṣa interpretation within a comparably brief interval, an account that would explain why richly prepared context is documented to make interpretation feel immediate and effortless to a well-oriented audience despite the underlying sign genuinely being multivalent at the moment it is first perceived, rather than context somehow preventing the ambiguity from existing at all.

Udāharaṇa — paṭāka disambiguated by narrative frame

A concrete worked case: paṭāka held in a raised position accompanied by a level gaze and a stationary body carries, absent further context, at minimum two broadly documented candidate readings — flag-signification and a certain kind of prohibitive gesture — among several other documented viśeṣa applications of the same base shape. Embedded in a dramatic sequence that has already established a battlefield setting through preceding narrative content, the same physical execution of paṭāka is read rapidly and confidently as flag-signification, the battlefield frame having pre-activated exactly the contextual expectation that narrows an audience's prior probability distribution over the hasta's possible meanings before the sign is even produced. Embedded instead in a sequence that has established a scene of a senior figure addressing a junior one, the identical physical execution is read instead as the prohibitive viśeṣa, with the specific narrative frame in each case doing the disambiguating work rather than any difference in the hasta's own physical execution, which, in this worked case, is held constant across both readings precisely to isolate the contribution of context alone.

Phala — why weak framing produces genuinely ambiguous, not merely under-appreciated, performance

This account carries a specific, sometimes underappreciated implication for how performance failures of legibility should be diagnosed. A hasta produced with technically flawless execution but insufficient surrounding narrative and tonal preparation is not, on this account, a case of an inattentive or insufficiently sophisticated audience failing to appreciate correct technique; it is a case in which the audience's own context-driven specialization process has genuinely insufficient material to work with, such that more than one documented viśeṣa reading remains live and unresolved in the audience's own perceptual processing, through no fault of the audience's attention or training. The corrective, on this account, is not addressed to the audience — asking them to attend more closely or to have studied the tradition more thoroughly — but to the performance's own preparation of context, a diagnostic distinction with direct practical consequences for where a teacher or director should look when a specific gestural moment is reported as unclear.

07

Exemplar Theory and the Memory of Particulars

Prototype theory, examined above, holds that a category is represented by a single abstracted central tendency, with new instances judged by their similarity to that one abstracted representation. Exemplar theory, developed as both a rival and, in later formulations, a complement to prototype theory, proposes instead that a category is represented by memory traces of the specific individual instances actually encountered, stored more or less as they were experienced, with a new instance classified by computing its similarity to this entire stored population of remembered particulars rather than to any single abstracted summary representation.

Exemplar theory gains empirical support from a range of findings that prototype theory alone handles less naturally, most notably that judgments about category membership are sensitive not only to a stimulus's overall typicality but to its specific similarity to particular remembered instances, including relatively unusual or atypical instances, in ways that would not be predicted if only a single abstracted prototype, stripped of information about specific individual instances, were being consulted. Current thinking in cognitive psychology increasingly treats prototype-based and exemplar-based representation not as mutually exclusive rival accounts but as two complementary components of a fuller memory system, with abstracted prototype-like representations supporting rapid, low-effort typical-case judgments, and more detailed exemplar-based memory traces supporting the finer-grained, context-sensitive judgments particular, less typical cases require.

This dual structure maps with some precision onto the way viśeṣa training appears to function. The sāmānya form corresponds to the abstracted, prototype-like component of the memory system: one stable, generalized representation, efficiently supporting rapid recognition of the hasta's basic identity regardless of context. The documented, individually named viśeṣa applications, each associated with its own specific dramatic context, correspond more closely to stored exemplars: specific, individually remembered instances of the general form's use, retained in a level of contextual detail an abstracted prototype alone does not carry, and consulted specifically when a new performance situation resembles one of these documented specific instances closely enough for exemplar-based similarity, rather than prototype-based typicality alone, to guide correct interpretation. A performer's full competence with a hasta, on this account, requires both components functioning together: the abstracted sāmānya prototype for rapid, general recognition, and a substantial stored library of specific viśeṣa exemplars for correctly handling the particular, contextually rich cases actual dramatic performance most often requires.

Lakṣaṇa — the specific empirical test that separates prototype-only from exemplar-inclusive accounts

The empirical case for retaining exemplar-level information, rather than treating a prototype alone as a sufficient account of category representation, rests on a specific class of findings worth stating precisely: old-item advantage effects, in which a specific previously encountered instance is classified more quickly and more accurately on a later encounter than an equally typical novel instance the subject has never actually seen before, despite both instances being, by hypothesis, equally close to the category's abstracted prototype and therefore predicted to be classified with equal ease under a prototype-only account. This old-item advantage — familiarity with the specific individual instance itself, over and above that instance's general typicality — is exactly what a pure prototype account, which by definition discards individuating information about specific encountered instances in favor of a single abstracted summary, cannot explain, and is exactly what exemplar theory, which retains individual instance memories rather than discarding them, predicts directly.

Prakriyā — how a dual prototype-and-exemplar system is thought to operate jointly

Current dual-process accounts of category representation propose a specific division of labor between the two components worth stating in operational terms. On encountering a new instance to be classified, the account holds that a rapid, comparatively low-effort comparison against the abstracted prototype occurs essentially automatically, sufficient by itself to correctly classify the considerable majority of instances that are reasonably typical of their category; a slower, more effortful comparison against the full population of stored individual exemplars is engaged more heavily, or is engaged as a secondary check, specifically for instances that are atypical, ambiguous, or that closely resemble a specific previously encountered exemplar in a way the abstracted prototype comparison alone would not capture. This graduated engagement — prototype comparison sufficient for the easy majority of cases, exemplar comparison recruited additionally for the harder minority — is what allows a dual system to combine the computational efficiency of prototype-based judgment with the finer-grained accuracy exemplar-based judgment provides for exactly the harder cases where that finer grain is actually needed.

Udāharaṇa — a performer's mnemonic library as a worked exemplar case

A concrete illustration of the exemplar component in trained performance practice: an experienced performer asked to recall how a specific viśeṣa application of paṭāka was executed in a specific, particularly memorable prior performance — a specific teacher's specific demonstration, or a specific moment in a specific performance the performer themselves once gave — is drawing on precisely the kind of individually retained exemplar memory exemplar theory describes, a memory considerably more detailed and more specifically bound to that one particular occasion than the abstracted sāmānya prototype alone could supply. This exemplar-level memory is documented, in the accumulated pedagogical experience of the tradition's own teaching lineages, to function as a valuable and frequently invoked resource precisely in the harder, more atypical, or more dramatically unusual cases exemplar theory predicts exemplar-level retrieval should be most heavily recruited for — a teacher correcting a student's unusual or ambiguous execution will often invoke a specific remembered instance, "as so-and-so performed it in such-and-such production," rather than restating the general sāmānya principle alone, exactly the pattern a dual prototype-and-exemplar account of expert memory would predict for handling a genuinely atypical or borderline case.

Phala — the training implication of a required exemplar library

If full viśeṣa competence genuinely depends on a substantial stored library of specific exemplars rather than on the sāmānya prototype alone, this carries a direct implication for the volume and variety of practical exposure a complete training must provide, distinct from the volume of purely sāmānya drilling addressed in earlier sections: a curriculum that provides extensive, well-structured sāmānya drilling but only a narrow, sparse sample of documented viśeṣa applications for each hasta would, on this account, produce a performer with a well-formed prototype but an impoverished exemplar library, correspondingly capable of confident recognition and production of the canonical general form but measurably less capable of correctly handling the atypical, context-rich particular cases exemplar-based judgment is specifically needed for. This gives a concrete, falsifiable prediction distinguishing prototype-sufficient and exemplar-requiring accounts of viśeṣa competence, and a specific, actionable design criterion for curriculum breadth: sufficient documented exemplar variety per hasta, not merely sufficient repetition of the sāmānya form.

08

Nāṭyadharmī and Lokadharmī: Definition and Function

Nāṭyadharmī and lokadharmī are a pair of technical terms the Nāṭyaśāstra uses to distinguish two different relationships a piece of stage behavior can bear to ordinary, unstylized worldly conduct. Lokadharmī names behavior on stage that closely resembles how the corresponding action or emotion actually appears in ordinary life, with comparatively little stylization or convention interposed between the depicted content and its everyday real-world counterpart: an actor miming the act of drinking water by bringing a cupped hand to the mouth in a manner closely resembling how a person actually drinks is behaving lokadharmī. Nāṭyadharmī names behavior on stage that is deliberately stylized according to theatrical convention, departing, often considerably, from how the corresponding content would appear in ordinary unstaged life, with the specific form of the departure fixed by accumulated dramatic convention rather than by resemblance to everyday behavior: a hasta held in a specific stylized configuration to signify an emotion, where no comparable hand configuration occurs in ordinary unstaged expression of that emotion, is nāṭyadharmī.

The distinction is documented as applying across the full range of theatrical elements, not confined to gesture alone: a costume closely resembling ordinary contemporary dress is lokadharmī, while a costume employing a stylized color-and-ornament code fixed by dramatic convention, with no equivalent code in ordinary dress, is nāṭyadharmī; a vocal delivery closely resembling how a person would actually speak under a given emotional state is lokadharmī, while a stylized, metrically heightened, or musically inflected delivery departing considerably from ordinary unstaged speech is nāṭyadharmī. Most actual performances documented by the tradition combine both modes rather than remaining exclusively in one, with the specific balance between them varying by dramatic genre, by the specific pravṛtti and vṛtti governing a given performance, and by the specific dramatic content being depicted at a given moment.

The functional purpose of maintaining both modes, rather than relying exclusively on one, is documented in the tradition's broader theoretical discussion of theatrical effect: content depicted in a purely lokadharmī manner risks being received as literal, unmediated reality rather than as art requiring aesthetic distance, while content depicted in a purely nāṭyadharmī manner risks losing legibility and emotional immediacy for an audience, since heavily stylized convention, however well-established within the tradition, does not carry the same immediate, unlearned emotional resonance that behavior closely resembling ordinary lived experience carries. The tradition's own practice of combining both modes within a single performance, adjusting their relative proportion according to dramatic need, is best understood as a deliberate management of this trade-off between aesthetic distance and emotional immediacy, rather than as an inconsistency in an otherwise unified system.

Lakṣaṇa — the axis of stylization stated precisely

The nāṭyadharmī/lokadharmī pair names a single continuous axis rather than a strict binary, with the two terms functioning as poles rather than as exhaustive, mutually exclusive bins. A given piece of stage behavior can be placed along this axis by asking a specific diagnostic question: does the behavior's form derive from resemblance to how the corresponding content actually appears outside a theatrical frame, or does its form derive from an agreed-upon theatrical code with no such resemblance requirement? Behavior deriving entirely from resemblance sits at the lokadharmī pole; behavior deriving entirely from code sits at the nāṭyadharmī pole; and the great bulk of actual documented stage practice sits somewhere between the two poles, combining a resemblance-based core with code-based heightening, rather than occupying either extreme in pure form.

Prakriyā — how a single depicted action moves along the axis

A single dramatic content item, such as weeping, can be staged at almost any point along this axis depending on directorial and genre choice, with the tradition documenting the full range as legitimate rather than treating one point on the axis as the sole correct rendering. Weeping staged close to the lokadharmī pole involves closely observed, naturalistic crying — irregular breath, genuine or convincingly simulated tears, an unstructured sequence of small movements. Weeping staged close to the nāṭyadharmī pole involves a stylized, rhythmically organized sequence of gestures and vocal patterns fixed by convention to signify grief, executed with a formal precision no genuinely grieving person's unstaged behavior would display. Intermediate stagings combine elements of both, retaining some naturalistic breath and facial engagement while organizing the accompanying hand and body movement according to stylized convention.

Udāharaṇa — costume as a fully worked case

Costume supplies a particularly clear worked case because its two poles are visually unambiguous. A performer dressed to resemble the ordinary attire of a specific historical or social milieu, with no symbolic color-coding beyond what that milieu's actual dress conventions would carry, is costumed lokadharmī. A performer whose costume instead follows a formal color-and-ornament code — a specific facial color-palette assigned by dramatic convention to a specific category of character, a specific headdress shape assigned to a specific character type, neither derived from how any actual person of that category historically dressed — is costumed nāṭyadharmī. Many documented performance traditions descending from this framework combine the two directly on a single performer: a broadly naturalistic base garment paired with a strongly coded, non-naturalistic facial or headdress treatment, precisely the intermediate combination the axis predicts as the most common actual case rather than the exception.

Phala — the trade-off stated as a design problem

Every choice of position along the lokadharmī-nāṭyadharmī axis trades one documented benefit against another rather than moving strictly toward improvement: shifting toward the lokadharmī pole buys immediate, low-effort emotional accessibility at the cost of the finer expressive precision only a coded vocabulary can supply, while shifting toward the nāṭyadharmī pole buys that precision at the cost of requiring an audience already equipped with the relevant learned associations. Treated as a design problem rather than as a matter of unexamined convention, the axis gives a director or performer an explicit variable to set deliberately for a given piece of dramatic content and a given audience, rather than an implicit stylistic default inherited unreflectively from genre habit.

09

Dual-Route Models of Action Recognition

Cognitive neuroscience's study of how an observer recognizes and interprets another person's bodily action has, over several decades of research, converged on a dual-route picture: that action recognition is supported by at least two functionally and anatomically distinguishable systems operating in parallel, rather than by a single unified recognition mechanism handling all observed action identically regardless of its nature.

The first route is a rapid, largely automatic system that appears to operate through a form of direct motor simulation: an observer's own motor system becomes active, in a manner correlated with the specific action being observed, essentially simulating the observed action within the observer's own motor representations without requiring the observer to consciously retrieve any learned symbolic association for what the action means. This route operates with particular efficiency for actions that closely resemble movements within the observer's own existing motor repertoire, since simulation depends on the observer's motor system being able to map the observed action onto an already-available internal motor representation of a similar or identical movement.

The second route is a slower, more clearly learned system operating through stored associative and symbolic memory: an observer recognizes an action not by simulating it directly but by matching the observed form against a previously learned association between that specific form and its conventional meaning, in essentially the same way a reader recognizes a written word not by simulating the act of producing that shape but by retrieving a learned association between the visual form and its meaning. This second route does not depend on the observed action closely resembling anything in the observer's own natural motor repertoire, since its operation rests on learned association rather than on direct motor resemblance, but it does depend on the observer having previously been exposed to, and having successfully learned, the specific convention linking that particular form to its intended meaning.

These two routes are not mutually exclusive alternatives an observer selects between, but appear, on the balance of the evidence, to operate simultaneously and in parallel for most observed actions, with their relative contribution to the observer's overall interpretation varying according to how closely the observed action resembles the observer's own natural motor repertoire on one hand, and how much prior specific learned association the observer brings to that particular conventional form on the other.

Lakṣaṇa — the specific behavioral markers that distinguish the two routes

Two behavioral markers separate simulation-route recognition from association-route recognition under controlled testing. The first is sensitivity to biomechanical plausibility: an observed movement that violates the physical constraints of a human body's actual joint articulation produces a measurable disruption in simulation-route processing, since the observer's own motor system cannot map a biomechanically impossible movement onto any available internal motor representation, whereas association-route processing, depending only on learned form-meaning pairing rather than on motor plausibility, shows no comparable disruption for an equally implausible but equally well-learned symbolic form. The second marker is sensitivity to prior individual exposure: simulation-route recognition of a biomechanically ordinary movement is largely unaffected by whether the observer has personally encountered that specific movement before, while association-route recognition of a genuinely arbitrary symbolic form is close to absent in an observer with no prior exposure to that specific convention, and improves systematically with the amount of prior exposure the observer has accumulated.

Prakriyā — how the two routes combine within a single observed action

For any given observed stage action, the two routes are best understood as each contributing a graded, weighted amount to the observer's overall interpretation rather than one route switching off entirely while the other operates alone. A movement with high biomechanical resemblance to ordinary action and a well-learned conventional meaning recruits both routes simultaneously and strongly, producing rapid, confident, doubly-supported recognition. A movement with low biomechanical resemblance but a well-learned conventional meaning recruits the association route strongly and the simulation route weakly, producing recognition that depends almost entirely on prior learning. A movement with high biomechanical resemblance but no learned conventional meaning at all recruits the simulation route strongly, producing an observer who can accurately describe what the movement physically resembles without being able to say what it is specifically intended to signify within the performance's own convention.

Udāharaṇa — a graded four-cell worked matrix

Crossing biomechanical resemblance against learned-convention strength produces four worked cells directly applicable to staged action. High resemblance, high convention: a naturalistically executed weeping sequence within a tradition the observer has studied, recognized rapidly through both routes at once. Low resemblance, high convention: a heavily stylized hasta sequence within a tradition the observer has studied, recognized confidently but with a measurably longer processing interval than the first cell, since the association route alone must complete its retrieval. High resemblance, low convention: the same naturalistic weeping sequence shown to an observer entirely unfamiliar with the specific performance tradition, still recognized as weeping through simulation alone despite the observer's unfamiliarity with the tradition's other conventions. Low resemblance, low convention: the same stylized hasta sequence shown to that same unfamiliar observer, recognized as a deliberate, meaningful gesture of some kind but not reliably identifiable as to its specific intended content, since neither route has sufficient material to work with.

Phala — why the two-route architecture rules out a single-mechanism account

A single unified recognition mechanism, lacking this two-route structure, would predict that recognition difficulty tracks only one variable — either resemblance alone or prior learning alone — rather than the documented independent contribution of both variables demonstrated by the four-cell pattern above. The fact that low-resemblance, high-convention material and high-resemblance, low-convention material produce measurably different recognition profiles from one another, rather than collapsing onto a single difficulty gradient, is itself the strongest evidence that two functionally separable mechanisms, rather than one, are jointly responsible for an audience's moment-to-moment recognition of staged action.

10

The Mirror Neuron System and Direct Resonance

The neural basis proposed for the rapid, simulation-based route of action recognition centers on what has come to be called the mirror neuron system, first identified through single-cell recording studies in the premotor cortex of macaque monkeys, which found individual neurons that fired both when the monkey itself performed a specific goal-directed action, such as grasping an object, and when the monkey merely observed another individual, whether monkey or human, performing that same action, without the observing monkey itself moving at all. This discovery was notable because it identified single cells whose activity did not distinguish between actively performing an action and merely watching someone else perform it, suggesting that observing an action recruits at least part of the same neural machinery that would be recruited to perform that action oneself.

Subsequent research, using neuroimaging methods in humans, has identified a broader network of brain regions, including areas of premotor cortex, inferior parietal cortex, and adjacent regions, that show comparable activity increases both during action execution and during action observation, commonly referred to collectively as a human mirror neuron system or action-observation network, though the precise cellular-level properties established in the original macaque single-cell recordings are considerably harder to confirm directly in humans given the more limited invasiveness of most human neuroscientific methods. The functional interpretation most commonly proposed for this system is that it supports rapid, automatic understanding of an observed action's goal by mapping the observed movement onto the observer's own motor repertoire for producing a similar movement, allowing the meaning of the action — what it is for, what state it expresses — to be recovered directly through this motor mapping, without requiring a separate, slower process of consciously retrieving a learned symbolic association.

Applied to lokadharmī performance, this account predicts, and available evidence on gesture comprehension across cultures broadly supports, that a stage action closely resembling an ordinary real-world movement is recognized rapidly and with comparatively little conscious effort by an observer whose own motor repertoire includes a closely matching movement, since the mirror system can map the observed stage action onto that pre-existing motor representation directly. This gives lokadharmī performance a specific, mechanistically grounded advantage in speed and immediacy of comprehension precisely because it deliberately preserves close resemblance to ordinary movement, keeping the fast simulation route maximally engaged rather than requiring the slower associative route to do most of the interpretive work.

Lakṣaṇa — goal-directedness as the operative trigger

Macaque premotor recordings established a specific and non-obvious property of mirror-neuron firing worth stating precisely: the relevant cells fire in response to a goal-directed action — grasping toward an object with the intention of retrieving it — but fire far more weakly or not at all in response to a superficially similar movement lacking a clear goal object, such as the same grasping motion performed toward empty space with no object present. This goal-directedness is what distinguishes the mirror mechanism from a simpler account in which any observed movement of a given shape would automatically trigger a matching motor response regardless of that movement's apparent purpose; the mechanism, as documented, is tuned specifically to purposive action rather than to movement shape in isolation.

Prakriyā — from single cells to the human action-observation network

Human neuroimaging work, using methods that measure aggregate activity across populations of neurons rather than the activity of individual cells, has identified a consistent set of cortical regions — premotor cortex, inferior parietal lobule, and portions of the superior temporal sulcus — that together show increased activity both when a person performs a goal-directed manual action and when that same person merely observes another individual performing it. This aggregate pattern is consistent with, though it does not by itself definitively prove, the presence of a human analogue to the macaque mirror-neuron mechanism, since aggregate activity increases could in principle arise from populations of neurons that are active during observation and separate, non-overlapping populations that are active during execution, rather than from the same individual cells being active during both, a distinction single-cell recording alone can resolve and non-invasive human neuroimaging generally cannot.

Udāharaṇa — the goal-directedness criterion applied to lokadharmī staging

The goal-directedness criterion has a direct, testable consequence for how lokadharmī staging should be constructed to maximize simulation-route engagement: a mimed action depicting a clear, recognizable goal — reaching for a specific object, drinking from a specific implied vessel — should, on this account, engage rapid simulation-based recognition more strongly than a movement of similar overall shape performed without any clearly implied goal object, even where both movements are equally naturalistic in their surface kinematics. This gives a specific, mechanistically grounded staging principle distinct from naturalism alone: lokadharmī legibility depends not merely on a movement looking like an ordinary movement, but on that movement clearly implying a specific goal an observer's own motor system can represent.

Phala — the specific limit of what mirror-based recognition explains

The mirror-neuron account explains rapid recognition of an action's physical goal — what the movement is directed at and what it is mechanically for — but does not, on its own, explain recognition of a movement's further symbolic or dramatic significance beyond that immediate physical goal, a further layer of meaning that depends on associative processing operating over and above simulation. A mimed drinking gesture is recognized as drinking through simulation alone; recognizing that this same drinking gesture, within a specific narrative moment, signifies a specific character's poisoning, grief, or celebration requires additional contextual and associative processing the mirror mechanism by itself does not supply.

11

Symbolic Association and the Slow Route

Where an observed action departs considerably from anything in the observer's own ordinary motor repertoire — as a heavily stylized nāṭyadharmī gesture, fixed by dramatic convention rather than by resemblance to any everyday movement, deliberately does — the rapid motor-simulation route has comparatively little natural material to work with, since there is no closely matching movement already available in the observer's motor repertoire for the observed action to be mapped onto. Recognition of such stylized, conventional forms instead depends substantially on a slower route operating through learned symbolic association: the observer must have previously encountered the specific conventional form in question, together with sufficient additional information — explicit instruction, repeated exposure within a consistent context, or explanation by another party — to have formed a stored association between that specific form and its conventional meaning, an association that must then be retrieved, rather than directly simulated, at the moment the form is observed again.

This associative route is considerably more effortful than direct motor simulation in at least two specific respects that have been documented in studies of symbolic and arbitrary sign comprehension more generally. First, it depends entirely on prior individual learning history rather than on any species-general or widely shared motor capacity, meaning two observers with different prior exposure to a given convention can differ substantially in how readily they recognize the same stylized form, in a way two observers would not be expected to differ nearly as much in recognizing an action that closely resembles ordinary movement and can therefore be read through direct simulation regardless of specific prior exposure to that particular theatrical tradition. Second, retrieval of a learned symbolic association is measurably slower, and more subject to interference and error under time pressure or divided attention, than the more automatic operation of the simulation-based route, meaning heavily nāṭyadharmī material generally requires either a more experienced audience or a more generous, less time-pressured pace of presentation than more heavily lokadharmī material does, if it is to be reliably comprehended.

This is not, on the available evidence, a deficiency of nāṭyadharmī convention relative to lokadharmī naturalism, but a genuine trade-off with its own compensating advantage: because a stylized, learned symbolic form does not depend on resembling anything in ordinary life, it is free to carry considerably more precise and more finely differentiated meaning than an unstylized, naturalistic action typically could. Ordinary, unstyled human movement provides a comparatively coarse channel for conveying fine emotional or narrative distinctions, since everyday movement was not designed or refined for this communicative purpose; a deliberately constructed symbolic vocabulary, precisely because it is unconstrained by any requirement to resemble ordinary behavior, can be refined and expanded, generation after generation, to draw far finer distinctions than naturalistic movement alone could reliably support, at the cost of requiring an audience to have previously learned the specific code being used.

Lakṣaṇa — the retrieval-cost signature of symbolic recognition

Studies of arbitrary sign comprehension, drawn from research on written word recognition and on constructed symbol systems more generally, document a specific retrieval-cost signature that separates symbolic recognition from simulation-based recognition: retrieval latency for a learned arbitrary form decreases as a function of that form's frequency of prior exposure, with highly familiar forms retrieved nearly as quickly as biomechanically simulable ones and rarely encountered forms retrieved considerably more slowly and with a measurably higher error rate. This frequency-sensitivity is a specific, quantifiable marker of associative retrieval and has no clear counterpart in simulation-based recognition, which depends on biomechanical resemblance rather than on frequency of prior exposure to that specific form.

Prakriyā — how repeated exposure converts effortful retrieval into near-automatic recognition

Repeated exposure to a given symbolic form is documented, across the general literature on skill acquisition for arbitrary associations, to shift retrieval from an effortful, consciously monitored search process toward a faster, less effortful process that shares some measurable properties with automatic recognition, though it remains distinguishable from genuine simulation-based automaticity by its continued sensitivity to the frequency and recency of prior exposure. A nāṭyadharmī gesture unfamiliar to a novice observer, requiring slow, effortful retrieval on first exposure, becomes progressively faster and less effortful to recognize across repeated exposures to the same tradition's conventions, without the underlying movement itself ever coming to resemble anything in ordinary life — the increasing speed reflects strengthening of the learned association, not any change in the movement's biomechanical resemblance to ordinary action.

Udāharaṇa — a precision-versus-speed worked comparison

A worked comparison between one lokadharmī and one nāṭyadharmī rendering of grief illustrates the precision-for-speed trade-off directly. Naturalistic weeping, recognized rapidly through simulation, reliably communicates the general category "distress" but does not, on its own naturalistic form alone, reliably distinguish grief over a death from grief over a betrayal from grief over a reversal of fortune, since ordinary unstaged weeping does not systematically vary its physical form according to which of these specific causes underlies it. A stylized, conventionally coded rendering of grief, by contrast, can and in documented practice does employ distinct, systematically differentiated gestural sequences for grief-from-death, grief-from-betrayal, and grief-from-reversal, each requiring its own separately learned association but together supplying a precision of distinction the naturalistic form alone cannot match.

Phala — audience experience level as the deciding variable in staging choice

Because symbolic-route recognition depends on cumulative prior exposure while simulation-route recognition does not, the same staging choice carries different practical consequences for audiences at different levels of prior exposure to a given tradition's conventions: heavy reliance on finely differentiated nāṭyadharmī coding rewards precision of meaning for an audience that has already accumulated the relevant associations, while imposing a comprehension cost on an audience that has not, a cost that naturalistic staging does not impose to nearly the same degree regardless of audience background.

12

Cross-Cultural Reception as Evidence for Two Routes

A dual-route account of action recognition generates a specific, testable prediction about how an audience entirely unfamiliar with a given performance tradition's specific symbolic conventions should respond to a performance drawn from that tradition: such an audience should retain reasonably good comprehension of the more heavily lokadharmī content, since the fast, simulation-based route on which lokadharmī comprehension chiefly depends does not require prior exposure to the specific tradition in question, while comprehension of the more heavily nāṭyadharmī content should be considerably poorer, since the slow, associative route on which nāṭyadharmī comprehension chiefly depends does require exactly the kind of tradition-specific prior learning such an audience by definition lacks.

This predicted pattern — partial rather than total comprehension, with the lokadharmī portion of a performance's content read considerably more successfully than the nāṭyadharmī portion — is broadly consistent with informal and documented reception evidence involving audiences encountering unfamiliar performance traditions for the first time, who are widely reported to correctly grasp a meaningful portion of a performance's basic emotional and narrative content on a first viewing, particularly where that content is conveyed through action closely resembling recognizable everyday behavior, while more specifically coded conventional elements remain largely opaque without further explanation or repeated exposure. A single-route account of gestural comprehension, treating all stage action as depending uniformly on either pure resemblance or pure learned convention, would not predict this specific graded, partial pattern of comprehension nearly as naturally as a dual-route account does.

The graded pattern also has a direct implication for how a performance intended to reach an unfamiliar audience might reasonably be constructed, distinct from how a performance intended for an audience already fluent in the tradition's full symbolic vocabulary might be constructed: increasing the relative proportion of lokadharmī content, or providing additional contextual framing sufficient to support at least a partial, approximate learned association for the performance's more heavily nāṭyadharmī elements, would be expected, on dual-route grounds, to measurably improve an unfamiliar audience's overall comprehension, without requiring that audience to undergo anything resembling the extended training a fully tradition-fluent audience member has typically undergone.

Lakṣaṇa — what would falsify the dual-route prediction

The prediction is stated precisely enough to be falsifiable: a single-route account predicts that comprehension difficulty for an unfamiliar audience should track a single underlying variable uniformly across all staged content, whereas the dual-route account predicts a specific dissociation, in which comprehension for one class of content — resemblance-based — remains high despite unfamiliarity, while comprehension for a second class — convention-based — drops sharply for that same unfamiliar audience. Finding that unfamiliar audiences comprehend both classes of content equally well, or comprehend both equally poorly, would count as evidence against the dual-route account; finding the documented dissociation instead supports it.

Prakriyā — the graded emotion-recognition literature as independent support

A separate and independently developed body of research on cross-cultural emotion recognition, concerned with facial expression rather than staged gesture specifically, has documented that certain basic emotional expressions are recognized above chance levels by observers from cultures with minimal or no prior contact with the expressing culture, a finding generally interpreted as evidence for a substantially universal, biologically shared component to at least some emotional expression, consistent with a simulation-route account of that shared component's recognition. This same literature documents that recognition accuracy for these expressions, while above chance across cultures, is nonetheless somewhat higher within a viewer's own culture than across cultural boundaries, a residual in-culture advantage consistent with an additional, smaller associative-route contribution layered on top of the largely universal simulation-route component.

Udāharaṇa — a structured comprehension experiment as it would need to be designed
