A genuine sāttvika bhāva — an actually occurring involuntary physiological response, such as real pallor or real trembling, produced through the specific psychophysiological techniques the tradition documents for cultivating them rather than merely mimed — occupies a distinct third position: its connection to the internal state it signifies is neither resemblance nor arbitrary convention but an actual causal connection, the visible physiological sign being genuinely produced by the same underlying state it signifies, making it, in Peirce's specific technical sense, an index. This is a mode of signification structurally different from both the iconic resemblance of lokadharmī gesture and the arbitrary convention of nāṭyadharmī gesture, and it is this indexical quality specifically that gives sāttvika bhāva the particular kind of evidential force and emotional weight the tradition's own theoretical writing consistently attributes to it, relative to gesture or vocal delivery alone, which remain capable of representing a state without any equivalent causal grounding in that state's actual occurrence.

Lakṣaṇa — placing all four channels on the same three-way grid

Extending the three-way analysis to the full caturvidha beyond gesture, symbol, and involuntary sign alone produces a fuller grid worth stating explicitly. Āṅgika abhinaya spans the icon-symbol axis directly, since a single gestural channel documents both lokadharmī, largely iconic, forms and nāṭyadharmī, largely symbolic, forms within itself. Vācika abhinaya is predominantly symbolic in its lexical content, since spoken words carry meaning through learned convention, while its melodic and rhythmic delivery can carry a secondary iconic component, a rising, agitated vocal contour resembling the acoustic profile of genuine agitation regardless of the specific words spoken. Āhārya abhinaya, costume and makeup, is predominantly symbolic in its coded conventions and predominantly iconic in its naturalistic base garments, mirroring the same lokadharmī-nāṭyadharmī split documented for costume convention generally. Sāttvika abhinaya alone among the four channels carries a substantial indexical component when genuinely, rather than merely mimetically, produced.

Prakriyā — how a single performance moment layers icon, index, and symbol

A single fully realized moment of performance typically activates more than one of the three mechanisms simultaneously across its different channels rather than relying on only one throughout. A performer depicting grief might combine a nāṭyadharmī, symbolically coded hasta sequence with genuine sāttvika pallor, indexically grounded in an actual physiological state, while simultaneously delivering dialogue whose lexical content is symbolic and whose vocal contour is iconically expressive of distress. The audience's overall impression of the moment's authenticity and legibility is built from this simultaneous layering, with the indexical component in particular documented as contributing a felt sense of genuineness the symbolic and iconic components alone, however skillfully executed, do not by themselves supply.

Udāharaṇa — genuine versus merely mimed sāttvika bhāva as the decisive test case

The distinction between an indexical sign and a merely iconic imitation of that same sign's visible surface is nowhere more consequential than in the specific case of sāttvika bhāva. A performer who has genuinely induced, through the tradition's documented psychophysiological technique, an actual physiological state of fear sufficient to produce real pallor and real trembling is producing an index: the visible sign is actually caused by the state it signifies. A performer who instead merely imitates the visible appearance of pallor and trembling, through surface muscular control alone, without the underlying physiological state actually occurring, is producing something closer to an icon of fear's visible appearance — resembling genuine fear's outward form without standing in the genuine causal relationship to fear that indexicality strictly requires. The tradition's own pedagogy, as documented, treats these as meaningfully different achievements, not interchangeable routes to the same visible result.

Phala — the audience's own implicit sensitivity to this difference

The claim that audiences register a difference between indexically genuine and merely iconically imitated sāttvika bhāva, even without possessing Peirce's technical vocabulary to name the difference explicitly, rests on the documented general finding that indexical signs are considerably harder to counterfeit convincingly than iconic ones, and that observers of physiological expression more broadly are frequently able to register subtle differences between genuine and simulated instances of a given visible expression without being able to articulate precisely what feature of the expression tipped their judgment.

16

Indexicality and the Involuntary Sign

Indexical signification depends specifically on an actual causal or physical connection between sign and object, rather than on resemblance or convention, and this has a direct consequence for how reliably an indexical sign can be trusted as evidence of the state it signifies: because an index is genuinely produced by its object, rather than merely representing it through resemblance or convention, an index is, in general, considerably harder to produce falsely or to counterfeit convincingly than an iconic or symbolic sign is, since producing a convincing false index typically requires actually triggering, by some other means, the same underlying causal process that ordinarily produces the genuine sign, rather than merely constructing a superficially similar-looking form.

This is precisely why sāttvika bhāva occupies the specific and demanding position it does within the tradition's own account of performance: unlike a hasta, which can be produced correctly through learned motor skill regardless of whether the performer is actually experiencing the corresponding internal state, or a costume, which can be donned regardless of the wearer's actual internal state, a genuine sāttvika bhāva, being indexical, is documented as requiring the performer to actually undergo, through disciplined technique, something closely resembling the genuine underlying physiological process ordinarily responsible for that sign, precisely because a merely mimed, non-indexical approximation of the visible sign, without the underlying causal process actually occurring, tends to be detectably different from the genuine indexical version, in ways an audience, whether consciously or not, is frequently documented as capable of registering.

This gives a further, mechanistically grounded explanation for a documented feature of the tradition's own pedagogy: the specific, extended psychophysiological training the tradition prescribes for sāttvika bhāva, oriented toward actually inducing the target physiological state through controlled recall, breath, and imaginative absorption rather than toward merely rehearsing an outward-facing visible shape, is precisely the kind of training indexicality requires and iconic or symbolic signification does not, since only the genuine underlying causal process, rather than any amount of skill at producing a superficially similar external shape, reliably yields the specific indexical quality sāttvika bhāva is documented to depend on for its distinctive communicative and aesthetic effect.

Lakṣaṇa — the counterfeit-resistance property stated precisely

Counterfeit-resistance, as a property of indexical signs, follows directly from the definition of indexicality rather than being a separately observed empirical add-on: since an index is, by definition, actually produced by its object, any process that produces the visible sign without the object's actual presence is, by definition, not producing a genuine index but only a resemblance to one, however perceptually convincing that resemblance might be. This is a logical consequence of the definition, not merely an empirical generalization that might turn out to admit exceptions; a sign is indexical exactly to the degree that its production is causally tied to its object, and to exactly that same degree, producing the sign without the object requires circumventing or substituting for that causal chain rather than merely reproducing its visible output.

Prakriyā — the physiological mechanisms the tradition's technique actually recruits

The specific physiological signs the tradition documents under sāttvika bhāva — pallor, trembling, horripilation, perspiration, voice-breaking, and their documented companions — are each produced, in their genuine involuntary form, through autonomic nervous system activity that is not, in the ordinary case, under direct voluntary muscular control in the way a hasta's finger position is. The tradition's documented psychophysiological technique for inducing these signs operates not by attempting to directly command the involuntary physiological system, which voluntary motor control cannot do, but indirectly, through controlled recall of emotionally significant personal memory, sustained imaginative absorption in the dramatic circumstance being portrayed, and specific breath patterns, each of which is capable, as ordinary emotional experience outside any performance context independently demonstrates, of triggering genuine autonomic activation as a natural consequence rather than through any direct voluntary override of the autonomic system.

Udāharaṇa — voice-breaking as a maximally legible worked case

Voice-breaking under genuine emotional strain supplies a particularly clear worked case of the counterfeit-resistance property, since the acoustic signature of a genuinely strained voice — involving involuntary changes to vocal fold tension and breath support driven by autonomic activation — is documented to differ measurably from a voluntarily produced imitation of vocal strain, in features of the acoustic signal a listener need not consciously analyze to register, even where the listener cannot articulate which specific acoustic feature tipped the judgment. A performer relying on genuine autonomic activation, achieved through the tradition's documented technique, produces the harder-to-imitate genuine signature; a performer relying on voluntary vocal-muscle mimicry alone produces a related but measurably distinguishable substitute.

Phala — the specific cost the tradition's technique is designed to pay

The extended, cumulative nature of sāttvika bhāva training, requiring years of disciplined practice in controlled emotional recall and imaginative absorption rather than the comparatively shorter motor-skill acquisition sufficient for reliable hasta production, is the direct, documented cost of pursuing indexical rather than merely iconic signification for this specific channel: a genuinely causal sign cannot be produced by shortcut, since any shortcut that bypasses the underlying causal process is, by the logic of indexicality itself, no longer producing that sign at all but only its counterfeit.

17

Pravṛtti: Regional Convention and Its Textual Basis

Pravṛtti is the term the Nāṭyaśāstra uses to classify performance according to regional convention, with the tradition documenting four principal pravṛttis, each associated with a specific geographic region and each carrying its own documented set of conventions governing costume, permissible dramatic content, characteristic speech register, and the relative prominence given to different elements of performance. These four pravṛttis are documented as historically and geographically grounded categories, reflecting genuine regional variation in how dramatic performance developed and was practiced in different parts of the subcontinent, rather than as a purely abstract theoretical typology invented independently of actual regional performance practice.

Each pravṛtti's documented conventions function as a coherent, internally consistent package rather than as an arbitrary or unrelated list of unconnected rules: the specific costume conventions, permissible content, and characteristic register documented for a given pravṛtti are consistently reported together as constituting a recognizable regional performance style, such that a performance adhering to one pravṛtti's full documented package of conventions would be recognizable, to an audience familiar with the scheme, as belonging to that specific regional tradition rather than to one of the other three, on the basis of this whole coherent bundle of conventions rather than on the basis of any single isolated feature considered alone.

Pravṛtti is documented as classifying performance along an axis independent of, though compatible with, the caturvidha abhinaya's own classification of performance by expressive channel: a single performance is understood as belonging to one pravṛtti while simultaneously deploying all four abhinaya channels in whatever combination the specific dramatic content requires, meaning pravṛtti and abhinaya are not competing or overlapping classificatory schemes but answer two genuinely different questions about a performance — which regional convention it follows, and through which expressive channels its content is being conveyed — that can in principle be answered independently of one another for any given performance.

Lakṣaṇa — the bundling criterion that defines a coherent pravṛtti

A pravṛtti is defined not by any single feature but by the specific documented co-occurrence of several features together, and the bundling criterion itself is worth stating precisely: a set of conventions constitutes a genuine pravṛtti, in the tradition's own account, only if those conventions are documented as consistently co-occurring in actual regional practice rather than being an arbitrary post-hoc grouping assembled for classificatory convenience. This bundling criterion is what allows the four-way pravṛtti scheme to function as a genuine empirical typology of regional practice, in principle falsifiable against actual documented performance, rather than as an a priori theoretical grid imposed on performance practice from outside.

Prakriyā — how a bundle of independently variable conventions becomes recognizable as one style

Costume convention, permissible content, and speech register are, considered individually, independently variable dimensions with no logical necessity binding them together — nothing about a specific costume convention logically requires a specific corresponding restriction on permissible content. Their consistent co-occurrence within a given pravṛtti is therefore a documented historical and cultural fact about how regional performance practice actually developed, rather than a logical entailment, and it is precisely this contingent, historically accumulated co-occurrence that allows a trained observer to infer several further features of a performance correctly from observing only one or two, in the same way a trained observer of any coherent regional cultural style can often correctly predict several further stylistic features from an initial, partial sample.

Udāharaṇa — inferring pravṛtti from a partial costume cue

A worked case illustrating the inferential power of bundled convention: an observer familiar with the four-pravṛtti scheme, shown only a specific documented costume element associated with one particular pravṛtti, can correctly predict, with above-chance accuracy, several further features of the performance that costume element belongs to — its characteristic speech register, its typically favored dramatic content — purely from the documented statistical association between that costume convention and those further features within the tradition's own recorded practice, without having directly observed those further features in this specific instance at all. This predictive power is itself evidence that the four pravṛttis are genuine, internally coherent bundles rather than arbitrary classificatory labels imposed after the fact.

Phala — why treating pravṛtti and abhinaya as independent axes matters analytically

Because pravṛtti and the caturvidha abhinaya answer genuinely independent questions, a complete formal description of any single performance moment requires specifying both: which pravṛtti's regional convention governs the moment, and which abhinaya channel or combination of channels is carrying the moment's content. Collapsing the two axes into one, treating pravṛtti merely as a stylistic variant of abhinaya rather than as an independent classificatory dimension, would obscure the documented fact that all four abhinaya channels are available for use within any of the four pravṛttis, each pravṛtti simply inflecting how those channels are specifically deployed rather than restricting which channels are available at all.

18

Schema Theory and Shared Expectation

Schema theory, developed within cognitive psychology substantially through early twentieth-century work on memory for stories and subsequently extended and formalized through later research on knowledge representation, holds that a considerable portion of everyday cognition depends on stored, generalized knowledge structures called schemas: structured representations, built up from repeated exposure to broadly similar situations, that specify the typical elements, typical sequence, and typical relationships characteristic of a familiar type of situation, and that allow a mind encountering a new but structurally similar situation to generate expectations about that new situation automatically, without needing to process every one of its features from scratch as though encountering something wholly unprecedented.

A schema, once formed, operates largely automatically and below the level of conscious deliberation: a person entering an unfamiliar restaurant for the first time nonetheless expects, correctly in the great majority of actual cases, to be seated, offered a menu, and asked to place an order in roughly that sequence, because a general restaurant-schema, extracted from the person's accumulated prior experience of restaurants in general rather than from any specific prior visit to this particular restaurant, generates this expectation automatically as soon as the situation is recognized as belonging to the general category "restaurant," well before any restaurant-specific detail has actually been observed. Schema theory further documents that schemas influence not only expectation but memory itself: information consistent with an active schema tends to be remembered more readily, and information inconsistent with an active schema is frequently distorted in memory to better fit the schema, a well-replicated finding with direct implications for how any shared cultural convention, once internalized as a schema, comes to shape not only what an observer expects but what an observer subsequently reports having actually seen.

Schemas of this kind are not confined to knowledge held by a single individual in isolation, but can be, and very often are, held jointly and consistently across an entire community of individuals who have each independently undergone broadly similar repeated exposure to the relevant class of situations, producing what is sometimes described as a shared or cultural schema: a generalized expectation-structure held in common across a whole population, such that any two members of that population, confronted independently with a new instance of the relevant familiar situation, would be expected to generate substantially similar expectations about it, without needing to communicate or negotiate those expectations with one another on that specific occasion.

Lakṣaṇa — the slot-and-filler structure of a schema

A schema, in its more formal cognitive-scientific specification, is standardly represented as a structure of slots, each slot corresponding to a typical element or role within the situation the schema represents, together with default fillers for each slot, representing the most typical value that slot takes absent specific contrary information. A restaurant schema's slots include a customer, a server, a menu, and an ordering sequence, each with default expectations attached; encountering a specific restaurant that fills these slots with atypical values — no server, for instance, in a self-service establishment — is registered as a deviation from default expectation requiring specific updating, rather than as evidence that no schema was operative at all.

Prakriyā — schema-consistent memory distortion as a documented mechanism

The schema-driven memory distortion effect documented in the lede operates through a specific, well-studied mechanism: at the moment of recall, a rememberer reconstructs a past event partly from genuine specific traces of that event and partly by filling in gaps in those traces using the relevant schema's default expectations, with the rememberer typically unable to distinguish, from the subjective experience of recall alone, which portions of the resulting memory derive from genuine specific trace and which portions derive from schema-driven default filling. This reconstructive process is documented to produce systematic, predictable memory errors specifically in the direction of the operative schema's defaults — a rememberer is measurably more likely to falsely recall a schema-typical element that was not actually present than to falsely recall a schema-atypical element that was not actually present.

Udāharaṇa — a shared cultural schema for a documented regional convention

Applied to a documented regional performance convention, an audience member who has repeatedly attended performances within a single pravṛtti's tradition accumulates a schema for that pravṛtti's characteristic costume, register, and content, such that a new performance within the same pravṛtti activates default expectations for these features automatically, before the performance's specific details have been directly observed. An audience member encountering a performance that violates one of these schema-driven default expectations — a costume element inconsistent with the pravṛtti the rest of the performance otherwise establishes — is documented, on general schema-theoretic grounds, to register this violation as a marked, salient departure requiring specific attention, precisely because it conflicts with an automatically generated default rather than because the audience member has consciously cross-checked the costume against an explicit mental list of that pravṛtti's documented conventions.

Phala — the shared-schema account of why regional convention functions communicatively at all

A shared cultural schema, held in common across an entire audience population through independently accumulated but convergent prior exposure, is what allows a performer to rely on a given pravṛtti's conventions to generate specific, predictable expectations in an audience without any need for explicit, moment-to-moment negotiation of what those conventions are; the schema does the communicative work of establishing a shared baseline automatically, provided the audience population has, in fact, accumulated sufficiently convergent prior exposure to that specific pravṛtti's conventions for the relevant shared schema to have formed in the first place.

19

Vṛtti: Dramatic Register and Its Textual Basis

Vṛtti is the term the Nāṭyaśāstra uses to classify dramatic tone or register, with the tradition documenting four principal vṛttis: bhāratī, the verbal register, drawing predominantly on spoken dialogue and verbal wit; sāttvatī, the grand and heroic register, associated with dignity, triumph, and elevated sentiment; ārabhaṭī, the vigorous and forceful register, associated with combat, violence, and confrontation; and kaiśikī, the graceful and delicate register, associated with romantic sentiment, gentleness, and refined beauty.

Each vṛtti is documented as drawing characteristically, though not exclusively, on particular combinations of the four abhinaya channels, with bhāratī drawing most heavily on vācika abhinaya, sāttvatī and ārabhaṭī each drawing predominantly on āṅgika abhinaya in markedly different registers from one another, and kaiśikī drawing jointly on āṅgika and āhārya abhinaya. Vṛtti is documented as a classification standing at the level of an entire dramatic passage or an entire play's dominant tonal character, rather than at the level of any single individual gesture or vocal delivery in isolation, meaning a given performance's assignment to a specific vṛtti governs the overall tonal register expected across that passage, within which the individual technical choices documented under the caturvidha abhinaya are then selected to be consistent with the governing vṛtti.

The four vṛttis are documented as being applied with differing appropriateness to different dramatic content: certain kinds of narrative material are documented as calling naturally for one vṛtti's characteristic register over the others, with dramaturgical guidance addressing which vṛtti is appropriate to which category of dramatic situation, and with skillful management of the transitions between vṛttis, as a play's content shifts from one kind of dramatic material to another, treated as a distinct and separately assessable dimension of a performance's overall craft.

Lakṣaṇa — register as a governing constraint on lower-level technical choice

Vṛtti's defining property, worth stating precisely against pravṛtti's regional-bundling structure and abhinaya's channel structure, is that it operates as a top-down governing constraint: once a passage's vṛtti has been established, the specific hasta, guṇa, and coded convention selected at any individual moment within that passage are selected from a subset consistent with the governing register, rather than freely from the full inventory the tradition documents overall. A gesture drawn from the vigorous, combat-associated register deployed within a passage otherwise governed by the graceful, romantic register would be registered by a trained audience as a tonal inconsistency, in the same way an unexpectedly harsh word choice registers as jarring within an otherwise gentle passage of ordinary speech.

Prakriyā — how a governing register is established and then maintained across a passage

A vṛtti is typically established at a passage's opening through an initial concentration of register-consistent cues across multiple channels simultaneously — an opening burst of vigorous āṅgika movement and forceful vācika delivery together establishing ārabhaṭī, for instance — after which the register, once established, requires comparatively less continuous reinforcement to be maintained, since an audience's own expectation, once the governing register has been set, continues to interpret subsequent, more ambiguous individual cues in a register-consistent direction by default. This front-loaded establishment followed by lighter maintenance is a documented efficient pattern distinct from a pattern of uniform, continuous signaling sustained at equal intensity throughout the entire passage.

Udāharaṇa — a documented vṛtti transition as a worked case

A dramatic sequence moving from an established ārabhaṭī passage of combat into a subsequent sāttvatī passage of triumphant resolution supplies a documented worked case of managed transition: the tradition's guidance treats this transition as requiring a deliberate intermediate handling, rather than an abrupt cut, typically involving a brief passage in which the vigorous, combat-associated cues of ārabhaṭī are progressively thinned while the dignified, elevated cues of sāttvatī are progressively introduced, allowing the audience's own register-tracking expectation to be smoothly re-set from one governing constraint to the other rather than experiencing the shift as an unmanaged tonal break.

Phala — why vṛtti mismanagement is documented as a distinct craft failure

Because vṛtti functions as a governing constraint over lower-level technical choice rather than as a mere decorative label, a performer capable of flawless individual hasta, guṇa, and coded execution can nonetheless produce a documented craft failure at the level of vṛtti specifically, by selecting individually correct technical elements that are nonetheless inconsistent with, or poorly transitioned into, the passage's governing register — a failure mode the tradition's own dramaturgical commentary treats as separately assessable from, and not reducible to, failures of individual technical execution.

20

The Circumplex Model and the Four Vṛttis

The circumplex model of emotion, proposed within affective science principally in the late twentieth century, offers an alternative to the more traditional approach of classifying emotional states into a short list of discrete named categories such as anger, fear, or joy. The circumplex model instead represents any given emotional state as a point located within a continuous two-dimensional space, with one dimension representing arousal, ranging from a state of low physiological and psychological activation to a state of high activation, and the second dimension representing valence, ranging from a strongly negative, unpleasant quality to a strongly positive, pleasant quality. On this model, a given discrete emotion word, such as fear or contentment, corresponds not to a wholly separate category but to a particular, characteristic region within this shared two-dimensional space, with emotions that are intuitively felt to be similar to one another generally occupying nearby regions of the space, and emotions felt to be quite different from one another occupying more distant regions.

Placed within this two-dimensional arousal-valence space, the four vṛttis occupy four distinct, well-separated regions rather than clustering together indistinguishably. Ārabhaṭī, the vigorous register associated with combat and violent confrontation, occupies a high-arousal region of the space, with its valence positioned toward the negative pole, consistent with the documented association between this vṛtti and threat, danger, and conflict. Sāttvatī, the grand and heroic register, likewise occupies a high-arousal region, but with valence positioned toward the positive pole rather than the negative, consistent with its documented association with triumph, dignity, and admiration rather than threat. Kaiśikī, the graceful and romantic register, occupies a considerably lower-arousal region than either ārabhaṭī or sāttvatī, with valence positioned strongly toward the positive pole, consistent with its documented association with gentleness and romantic pleasure. Bhāratī, the verbal register, is comparatively unfixed in its placement within this space relative to the other three, since spoken dialogue can in principle carry almost any combination of arousal and valence depending on its specific content, a flexibility consistent with bhāratī's documented breadth relative to the other three, more tonally specific vṛttis.

This dimensional placement gives a precise, quantifiable account of why the four vṛttis are experienced as clearly distinguishable from one another despite all four belonging to a single shared underlying classificatory scheme: each occupies a genuinely distinct and well-separated region of the same underlying two-dimensional space every human emotional experience is understood, on the circumplex model, to be positioned within, rather than the four vṛttis constituting four entirely unrelated, incommensurable categories bearing no systematic relationship to one another or to the broader structure of human affective experience.

Lakṣaṇa — arousal and valence defined independently of any specific emotion label

Arousal and valence are defined, within the circumplex framework, independently of any specific named emotion, which is what allows the two dimensions to serve as a genuinely general coordinate system rather than a restatement of the emotion labels themselves. Arousal is standardly indexed through physiological measures — heart rate, skin conductance, respiration rate — that track activation level without themselves specifying whether that activation is pleasant or unpleasant. Valence is standardly indexed through separate measures of approach-versus-avoidance behavioral tendency and subjective pleasantness ratings, independent of activation level. A state can be high-arousal-positive, high-arousal-negative, low-arousal-positive, or low-arousal-negative, with all four combinations independently documented and behaviorally distinguishable, confirming that the two dimensions are genuinely orthogonal rather than one being a disguised restatement of the other.

Prakriyā — locating a vṛtti's characteristic region from documented content associations

A vṛtti's placement within the arousal-valence space is derived, on the account developed here, not from any direct physiological measurement of performers or audiences but from the documented pattern of dramatic content each vṛtti is associated with, combined with the independently established finding that specific categories of real-world situations — physical threat, social triumph, romantic tenderness — reliably produce specific, measurable regions of arousal and valence in studies of ordinary human emotional response to those same situation categories outside any performance context. Ārabhaṭī's documented association with combat content places it, by this route, in the same high-arousal-negative region independently documented for genuine threat-response in the general affective-science literature, rather than the placement being an assumption imported without independent grounding.

Udāharaṇa — a worked comparison of sāttvatī and ārabhaṭī at matched arousal

Sāttvatī and ārabhaṭī supply a particularly instructive worked comparison precisely because the circumplex model predicts they should be close to one another on the arousal dimension while being well separated on the valence dimension, a specific, falsifiable prediction distinct from a vaguer claim that the two registers simply "feel different." Documented content associated with each register is consistent with this prediction: both involve heightened activation — raised voice, vigorous movement, urgent tempo — but sāttvatī's associated content, triumph and dignified resolve, is consistently positively valenced, while ārabhaṭī's associated content, violent confrontation and danger, is consistently negatively valenced, exactly the matched-arousal, opposed-valence pattern the model predicts rather than either register being simply "more intense" than the other along a single undifferentiated intensity scale.

Phala — why a two-dimensional model outperforms a simple intensity ranking

A cruder, single-dimensional account of the four vṛttis, ranking them merely by overall intensity from calmest to most intense, would be unable to distinguish sāttvatī from ārabhaṭī at all, since both are documented as high-intensity registers; only a model with at least two independent dimensions can capture the documented fact that these two registers are simultaneously similar in one respect, activation level, and sharply distinct in another, emotional quality, giving the circumplex model genuine explanatory power beyond a merely descriptive relabeling of four traditionally named categories.

01

Sāmānya and Viśeṣa: Definition and Function

Sāmānya and viśeṣa are a pair of technical terms the Nāṭyaśāstra uses to distinguish two levels at which any single item of performance technique can be specified: the general level, at which a hasta, a guṇa, or a costume convention is described in a form broad enough to be usable across many different dramatic situations, and the particular level, at which that same general form is modified, narrowed, or combined with additional cues to fit one specific dramatic moment. A hasta such as paṭāka — four fingers extended and joined, thumb bent across the palm — has a documented sāmānya description that says nothing about which of its many possible meanings is in play at a given point in a performance; it is a shape, taught and named as a shape. The same hasta's viśeṣa applications are each a specific, named use of that shape in a specific context — held horizontally to signify a flag, moved in a particular arc to signify a cheek being stroked, held vertically near the chest to signify a certain kind of prohibition — with the tradition documenting a substantial number of such particular applications for this single general form.

The relationship between the two levels is not arbitrary substitution but systematic derivation: every documented viśeṣa application is recognizably a modification of its sāmānya base rather than an unrelated new shape, and a student who has learned the sāmānya form correctly can, in principle, recognize a new viśeṣa application as a variant of a form already known, rather than needing to learn it as an entirely unconnected unit. This derivational relationship is what makes the sāmānya/viśeṣa distinction a genuine economy of representation: instead of a performer needing to memorize hundreds of unrelated shapes, one for every dramatic situation the tradition has ever catalogued, a comparatively small number of sāmānya forms generates the full documented range of viśeṣa applications through a limited, learnable set of modification principles — direction of movement, speed, accompanying facial expression, combination with a second hasta, and positioning relative to the body.

This same two-level structure is documented as applying across all four abhinaya channels, not only to hasta. A guṇa such as mādhurya, sweetness, has a sāmānya vocal quality documented independently of any specific dramatic content, and a range of viśeṣa applications in which that same quality of voice is deployed for a specific character, a specific line, or a specific emotional register. A sāttvika bhāva such as romāñca, horripilation, has a sāmānya physiological description — the visible standing of body hair — and viśeṣa applications distinguishing the horripilation of fear from the horripilation of erotic excitement or religious awe, each requiring the base physiological sign to be combined with different accompanying cues before its specific intended meaning becomes legible to an audience. The distinction, in other words, is not a classificatory convenience specific to gesture, but a general design principle the Nāṭyaśāstra applies uniformly across everything it classifies as abhinaya.

Lakṣaṇa — the defining boundary of the pair

Formally stated, sāmānya is the invariant residue of a technique that survives across every one of its documented uses, while viśeṣa is whatever is added to that invariant residue on any single occasion of use. This has a testable consequence: if a feature of a hasta's execution changes from one viśeṣa application to the next while the underlying identity of the hasta remains recognized as "the same hasta," that feature belongs to viśeṣa; if a feature is constant across every documented application, it belongs to sāmānya. Applied to paṭāka, the joined, extended four fingers and bent thumb are constant across every documented use and therefore belong to sāmānya, while the angle of the wrist, the speed of the movement, and the accompanying gaze direction vary by application and therefore belong to viśeṣa. This operational test is what allows the distinction to function as a genuine analytic tool rather than as a loose intuitive label applied after the fact to whatever a teacher happens to demonstrate first.

A further boundary condition worth stating precisely: sāmānya is not simply "whatever is taught first" as a pedagogical accident of sequencing, but is specifically the invariant structural core independently of teaching order. It is possible, in principle, for a teacher to introduce a viśeṣa application before the sāmānya form has been isolated and named — a beginner might first encounter paṭāka only in its flag-signifying viśeṣa use, embedded in a specific dramatic scene, without yet having the base form abstracted out and drilled on its own. The distinction between sāmānya and viśeṣa is therefore logically prior to, and independent of, any specific curriculum's sequencing: it identifies what is invariant and what is context-added in the technique itself, a fact about the technique's own internal structure rather than a fact about how or when any particular teacher chooses to present it.

Prakriyā — how the general is built from the particular, and the particular derived from the general

The tradition's own pedagogical practice, as documented and as transmitted through unbroken guru-śiṣya lineages into the present, moves in both directions across this distinction depending on the stage of training. In the early stage, the sāmānya form is presented as a fixed target: the teacher demonstrates paṭāka's canonical shape, the student repeats it until the shape is stable and can be produced correctly without visual reference to the teacher's hand, and only once this stability is achieved does instruction proceed to viśeṣa variation. In the later stage of training, once a substantial viśeṣa vocabulary has been built up across many hastas, the direction of instruction can reverse: an advanced student may be shown an unfamiliar viśeṣa application and asked to identify, unprompted, which sāmānya form underlies it, a diagnostic exercise that tests whether the abstraction has genuinely been internalized as a re-usable general category or merely rehearsed as a list of disconnected specific instances.

This bidirectional movement — general to particular in early training, particular to general as a diagnostic in later training — is itself evidence that the sāmānya/viśeṣa distinction is not a merely descriptive label applied by scholars after the fact to organize a body of technique that would function identically without the label, but an operative structural principle actually used within the tradition's own transmission practice to test whether learning has proceeded correctly. A student who cannot perform this reverse derivation — who can produce a specific viśeṣa application only by rote repetition of that one specific sequence, without being able to relate it back to its sāmānya base or to derive a new, previously undemonstrated viśeṣa application from that base — is, by the tradition's own working standard, considered not yet to have mastered the hasta in question, however accurately any one memorized sequence is reproduced.

Udāharaṇa — a worked case across two channels

Consider the guṇa mādhurya alongside the hasta paṭāka as a paired worked example, since holding one channel constant against a channel already introduced clarifies how uniformly the sāmānya/viśeṣa logic transfers. Mādhurya's sāmānya description specifies a vocal quality independent of any lexical content: a rounded, unforced tone with comparatively narrow pitch excursions and a softened onset to each syllable, documented as achievable, in principle, while vocalizing any text whatsoever, dramatic or not. Its viśeṣa applications each combine this constant vocal quality with a specific further constraint — mādhurya deployed for a young female character's endearment differs from mādhurya deployed for a mother's tenderness toward a child in the specific further modulation of tempo and in the specific accompanying facial engagement, even though the base vocal quality, the rounded unforced tone itself, remains the shared invariant across both. A performer asked to demonstrate mādhurya "in general," with no dramatic content specified, is being asked for the sāmānya form; a performer asked to demonstrate mādhurya "as a mother speaks to a sleeping infant" is being asked for one specific viśeṣa derivation of that same form.

Where the two channels' worked examples diverge instructively is in the visibility of the underlying invariant to an outside observer. Paṭāka's sāmānya shape is directly, visually inspectable — a teacher can hold the hand still and point to the constant features. Mādhurya's sāmānya quality is auditory and temporally extended rather than instantaneously inspectable, and correspondingly harder to isolate for a beginning student to imitate from a single demonstration, since an invariant that cannot be held still for inspection is measurably harder to abstract from variable instances than one that can be paused, pointed at, and repeatedly compared feature by feature against a still-held target.

Phala — what the distinction buys the tradition, and what it costs

The primary documented benefit of maintaining the sāmānya/viśeṣa distinction as an explicit, named part of the technical vocabulary, rather than leaving the general/particular relationship implicit and unlabeled as many technique traditions do, is that it gives the tradition's own internal pedagogy a vocabulary for diagnosing a specific, common failure mode: the student who has memorized a large viśeṣa vocabulary without ever forming the underlying sāmānya abstraction. Because the terms exist and are explicitly taught, a teacher can name this failure precisely — pointing out that a student "has the viśeṣa but not the sāmānya" — rather than relying on a vaguer, less actionable diagnosis such as "insufficiently fluent" or "not yet ready." This precision in diagnosis is, on the account given in sections two through five below, directly continuous with what contemporary categorization science identifies as the difference between rote instance-memorization and genuine category abstraction, a distinction that research in that field also found necessary to name explicitly before it could be reliably assessed and trained for.

The cost of maintaining the distinction, documented candidly within the tradition's own pedagogical commentary rather than only by outside observers, is that early training organized strictly around isolated sāmānya drilling can, if pursued for too long without eventual viśeṣa application, produce a performer whose execution is technically stable but expressively inert — capable of the correct shape but not yet of using that shape to mean anything specific in a live dramatic context. This is not evidence against the sāmānya/viśeṣa distinction itself but a documented caution about how long isolated sāmānya training should be sustained before viśeṣa application is introduced: too short a period of isolated drilling leaves the general form unstable and prone to distortion under the pressure of contextual variation, while too long a period leaves a technically correct performer with nothing yet to say.

02

Categorization as a Cognitive Universal

Categorization is the cognitive operation of grouping distinct particular things under a shared general label, and of subsequently retrieving or producing a specific instance appropriate to a specific situation from that general label. It is one of the most extensively studied problems in cognitive psychology because it is not a specialized or occasional mental operation confined to unusual tasks, but a precondition for almost every other act of perception, memory, and reasoning a mind performs. A person encountering an unfamiliar object does not, in the ordinary case, analyze it from first principles as an unprecedented physical form; the object is recognized as an instance of an already-known category — a chair, a doorway, a raised voice — within a fraction of a second, and that categorization is what allows the rest of cognition, including planning an appropriate response, to proceed at all.

The practical necessity of categorization becomes clear by considering what its absence would require. A mind that treated every new instance of a familiar type of object or event as wholly unprecedented would face an impossible computational burden: virtually infinite variation exists among the physical instances that fall under any single everyday category, no two chairs, no two spoken syllables, and no two hand gestures ever being physically identical down to the last measurable detail, and a mind unable to abstract past this variation to a shared general category would be unable to apply anything learned from past experience to a new instance, however similar that instance in fact is to instances already encountered. Categorization is the cognitive solution to this problem: it allows a finite amount of stored general knowledge to be applied usefully to an effectively unlimited number of specific instances, each recognized as falling under one of a comparatively small number of known categories despite no two instances being identical.

Categorization research further distinguishes between the storage of the general category itself and the recognition of a new instance as belonging to it, since these are, on investigation, somewhat separable cognitive operations that can be studied and even selectively impaired independently of one another. Forming a stable, well-organized general category from repeated exposure to varied instances is one operation; correctly and rapidly classifying a brand-new instance against an already-formed category is a related but distinct operation, since a category can be well-formed in memory and yet a new borderline instance still take longer, or be more prone to error, to classify correctly than a highly typical instance would. This separation between category formation and instance classification maps closely onto the separation the Nāṭyaśāstra's own sāmānya/viśeṣa terminology draws between the general form, established once through direct training, and the particular application, requiring a further, situation-specific act of correct classification and modification each time it occurs.

Lakṣaṇa — defining categorization precisely against neighboring operations

It is worth being precise about what categorization is not, since the term is sometimes used loosely to cover any act of mental grouping whatsoever. Categorization in the technical sense used throughout the cognitive-scientific literature refers specifically to the assignment of a particular instance to one member of an already-established, reusable set of general classes, distinguishing it from at least two neighboring operations: simple discrimination, the ability merely to tell two instances apart without necessarily assigning either to any named general class, and one-off individuation, the recognition of a single unique particular as itself, with no claim to generality across other instances at all. A performer distinguishing two slightly different executions of the same hasta from one another is engaged in discrimination; a performer recognizing a specific viśeṣa application as an instance of a known general category is engaged in categorization proper; and an audience member forming a memory of one specific unrepeated performance moment, without any claim that this moment instantiates a recognized general type, is engaged in individuation. All three operations occur constantly in live performance and reception, but only categorization — the third of the three in isolation would leave nothing transferable to future encounters — is what supports the kind of cumulative, transferable skill the tradition's training methods are built to produce.

Prakriyā — the developmental and comparative evidence for categorization as universal

The claim that categorization is a cognitive universal rather than a specialized skill confined to particular cultures, particular training regimes, or particular species is supported by two independent bodies of evidence documented within cognitive and developmental psychology. Developmental research finds that infants, well before the acquisition of language and well before any formal instruction of any kind, already demonstrate measurable sensitivity to category structure — for instance, showing renewed attention, a documented marker of novelty detection, when a sequence of visually similar items is interrupted by an item from a different category, even when the infant has received no explicit teaching about either category and the categories in question are not named in any language the infant has yet acquired. This finding indicates that some capacity for extracting shared structure across varied instances is operative extremely early in cognitive development, prior to and largely independent of formal instruction, rather than being a skill that must be painstakingly built from nothing by explicit teaching alone.

Comparative research across non-human species provides a second, independent line of evidence: categorization behavior, assessed through tasks in which an animal must respond consistently to novel instances of a trained category while withholding that same response from instances of a contrasting category, has been documented across a wide range of species with very different neural architectures and very different natural ecologies, from pigeons trained on visual discrimination tasks to primates trained on more complex hierarchical category structures. The breadth of this cross-species evidence indicates that some form of categorization capacity is not a specifically human cognitive achievement bound up with language, but a considerably more general computational solution to the problem of extracting reusable structure from variable sensory experience, a solution that human cultural training systems — including the Nāṭyaśāstra's own sāmānya/viśeṣa pedagogy — can subsequently recruit, refine, and deliberately optimize far beyond whatever baseline categorization capacity is present without training.

Udāharaṇa — categorization failure as diagnostic evidence for categorization's normal operation

One of the more informative sources of evidence for how categorization ordinarily functions comes, perhaps counterintuitively, from documented cases in which it fails to function normally. Certain forms of acquired brain injury, most informatively injury localized to specific regions of temporal and frontal cortex, have been documented to selectively impair categorization while leaving other, superficially similar cognitive capacities comparatively intact — a patient may retain the ability to perceive an object's individual visual features accurately, and may retain considerable general knowledge acquired before the injury, while nonetheless showing a specific, measurable difficulty correctly classifying novel instances against previously known categories, or a specific difficulty forming stable new categories from post-injury experience. This double dissociation between intact perception and intact prior knowledge, on the one hand, and impaired categorization specifically, on the other, is exactly the kind of evidence cognitive neuroscience treats as indicating that categorization is a genuinely separable cognitive operation with its own dedicated, damageable neural substrate, rather than being merely an automatic byproduct of perception or memory functioning normally elsewhere in the brain.

Phala — the pedagogical stakes of treating categorization as foundational

If categorization is, as this evidence indicates, a genuine, separable, foundational cognitive operation rather than an automatic byproduct of other capacities, then a training system that deliberately and explicitly cultivates strong sāmānya abstraction — rather than relying on incidental exposure to accumulate it as an unplanned side effect of viśeṣa practice — is training something with independent cognitive reality and independent cognitive value, not merely organizing curriculum content for convenience of presentation. This gives a direct, non-metaphorical justification, external to the tradition's own internal rationale, for the emphasis the Nāṭyaśāstra places on the sāmānya form as a distinct object of training in its own right: the tradition is, on this account, training a genuine and independently identifiable cognitive faculty, and doing so through a method — isolated repetition of an invariant target prior to variation — that independently developed cognitive-scientific research on category learning has separately identified as effective for exactly this purpose, using populations, materials, and methods with no historical connection whatsoever to the śāstric tradition itself.

03

From Classical to Prototype Theories of Categories

For much of the twentieth century, the dominant assumption in both philosophy and early cognitive psychology was what is now called the classical theory of categories: the view, traceable to Aristotelian logic, that every category is defined by a fixed list of necessary and sufficient features, such that any object either possesses all the required features and is unambiguously a member of the category, or lacks at least one required feature and is unambiguously excluded from it, with no intermediate or graded cases permitted by the logic of the definition itself.

This classical assumption was substantially overturned by a body of experimental work conducted principally in the nineteen-seventies, which demonstrated that ordinary human categories, as they actually function in memory and judgment, very rarely behave as the classical theory predicts. Subjects asked to rate how good an example a given item is of a familiar category consistently produce graded ratings rather than uniform yes-or-no judgments — a robin is reliably judged a more typical bird than a penguin, even though both are, by any classical checklist definition of "bird," equally members of the category — and subjects are measurably faster and more accurate at verifying category membership for highly typical instances than for atypical but still genuine instances, an effect that a strict all-or-nothing classical category structure would not predict at all, since classical membership does not admit of degree.

These findings led to prototype theory, which holds that categories are represented in memory not as a checklist of defining features but as a prototype: an abstracted representation of the most typical, central member of the category, built up from the statistical regularities across many encountered instances, with judgments about new or borderline instances made by assessing their similarity to this central prototype rather than by checking them against a fixed feature list. A category, on this account, has a graded internal structure — some members close to the prototype and judged highly typical, others more peripheral and judged less typical — rather than a sharp, uniform boundary separating members from non-members. Subsequent research has refined and partially qualified prototype theory in various ways, including proposals that categories are represented through stored specific exemplars rather than, or in addition to, a single abstracted prototype; but the central empirical finding that motivated the shift away from the classical theory — that category membership and typicality are graded rather than uniform — has remained robust across this subsequent refinement.

Lakṣaṇa — the specific empirical signatures that separate the two theories

Two experimental signatures in particular are worth stating precisely. The first is the typicality-rating signature: when subjects are asked to rate exemplars of a category on a numerical scale of how good or representative an example each is, ratings distribute continuously rather than clustering at only the two extremes a strictly classical, checklist-based category would predict. The second is the reaction-time signature: when subjects are asked to verify, as quickly as possible, whether a given item belongs to a given category, response latencies are shorter and error rates lower for items independently rated as highly typical than for items independently rated as less typical but still genuine members, with this latency difference persisting even when the classical, checklist-based membership status of the two items is identical. Both signatures indicate that whatever representation subjects are actually consulting when they make these judgments has graded internal structure, rather than the flat, undifferentiated structure a classical checklist definition would produce.

Prakriyā — how the classical theory's specific predictions were tested and failed

The classical theory's decisive vulnerability, from an experimental standpoint, was that it made a strong and directly falsifiable prediction: if category membership is genuinely all-or-nothing, determined by a fixed feature checklist, then every member of a category ought to be equally good an example of it, differing at most in how quickly a given feature can be visually confirmed, but not in any more fundamental sense of "how much" a given member belongs to the category. Researchers exploited exactly this prediction by constructing category-membership judgment tasks in which classical membership was held constant — every tested item was, by any reasonable checklist definition, an equally genuine member of the category — while typicality was allowed to vary freely, as independently established by a separate group of raters. Under the classical theory's own prediction, response time and accuracy on these tasks should not have varied with typicality at all, since typicality was defined as orthogonal to strict membership; the finding that response time and accuracy did vary systematically and considerably with typicality, despite membership itself being held constant, is what constituted the direct empirical falsification of the classical theory's core claim, rather than merely a finding that sat awkwardly alongside it.

Udāharaṇa — the furniture and bird category studies as worked cases

The category "furniture" provides one of the more extensively documented worked cases in this literature. Items such as chair and sofa are consistently rated, across independent groups of raters, as highly typical furniture, while items such as lamp or telephone, though frequently included by subjects within a broadly construed "furniture" category in some studies and excluded in others, are rated considerably less typical when included at all, and items such as stove or fan occupy a genuinely graded, contested middle ground on which raters themselves disagree. This graded disagreement at the category's periphery, rather than a sharp and universally agreed boundary, is itself a further piece of evidence against the classical theory, since a genuine checklist-based definition, correctly applied, ought to produce consistent classification of borderline cases across raters rather than the documented rater-to-rater disagreement actually observed. The bird category provides the second classic worked case, with the specific documented finding that robin, sparrow, and similar small perching birds are consistently rated more typical than ostrich, penguin, or chicken, despite all being, by any ordinary biological checklist, equally genuine members of the category "bird."

Phala — why the shift from classical to prototype theory matters beyond psychology itself

The shift from classical to prototype theory matters here for a specific reason beyond its intrinsic interest within cognitive psychology: it supplies an independently validated, empirically grounded theoretical vocabulary — prototype, typicality gradient, graded category membership — that turns out to describe, with considerable precision, a structural feature the Nāṭyaśāstra's sāmānya/viśeṣa distinction was already implicitly built around many centuries before this vocabulary existed in its present form. On grounds entirely internal to cognitive psychology's own experimental history, graded prototype structure is not a special or unusual feature confined to invented laboratory categories, but a documented, general property of how human categorization actually operates across the ordinary categories of everyday cognition — which is what makes it a non-arbitrary point of comparison for a deliberately constructed technical vocabulary such as the hasta inventory, rather than a superficial analogy chosen for rhetorical convenience.

04

Prototype Structure in the Sāmānya Form

The sāmānya form of a hasta or a guṇa functions, on inspection, precisely as a prototype functions in the technical sense established by prototype theory: it is the single, centrally taught version of the category, referenced whenever the category is named in isolation, and it is the version every documented viśeṣa application is understood as a graded modification of, rather than each viśeṣa application constituting an independent, unrelated category with its own separate name and its own separate training requirement.

This has a direct, measurable consequence for the cognitive economy of training. If each viśeṣa application of paṭāka were, in fact, stored and retrieved as an entirely separate category unconnected to any shared prototype, a performer would need to independently memorize as many distinct motor and perceptual categories as the tradition documents specific applications for — a number considerably larger, across the full hasta inventory, than the number of base hand-shapes themselves. Because the viśeṣa applications are instead organized around a shared sāmānya prototype, the actual memory burden is very much smaller: one prototype, plus a comparatively short list of modification principles — angle, speed, accompanying facial cue, combination with a second hasta — that recombine to generate the full documented range of specific meanings. This is the same graded, prototype-centered economy prototype theory documents for everyday categories such as "bird" or "chair," applied here to a domain-specific, deliberately constructed technical vocabulary rather than to a category acquired informally from unstructured exposure.

A further parallel concerns typicality effects. Just as subjects are faster and more accurate judging a robin than a penguin as a bird, a trained performer and a trained audience member are, on the available anecdotal and pedagogical evidence surrounding hasta recognition, faster and more confident recognizing a hasta held in its canonical sāmānya position and orientation than recognizing the same hasta in a more peripheral, heavily modified viśeṣa application, particularly one that combines several modification principles simultaneously. This typicality gradient is precisely what a prototype-structured category predicts and a classical, all-or-nothing category structure does not, and it gives a further reason, beyond simple economy of memorization, for training to begin with the canonical sāmānya form: the canonical form is not merely easier to learn first, but is also, once learned, the fastest and most reliably recognized point of reference against which every more peripheral variation is subsequently read by an audience.

Lakṣaṇa — distinguishing an engineered prototype from an emergent one

A distinction worth making explicit, and one the tradition's own account does not itself need to make since it is concerned with practice rather than with categorization theory, is between an emergent prototype and an engineered one. The prototypes prototype theory was originally developed to describe — "bird," "furniture," "fruit" — are emergent: no single authority designed the robin to be the most typical bird, and the graded structure of the category arose unplanned from the accumulated statistical regularities of ordinary experience across a whole population of language users. The sāmānya form of a hasta, by contrast, is an engineered prototype: it was, at some point in the tradition's textual and pedagogical history, deliberately fixed, named, and prescribed as the canonical reference form, rather than emerging unplanned from unstructured population-level exposure. This is not a claim that the hasta system spontaneously acquired prototype structure the way "bird" did, but the stronger and more specific claim that a deliberately engineered technical vocabulary, designed centuries before prototype theory existed, independently arrived at the same graded, single-reference-point structure that prototype theory subsequently discovered to be characteristic of naturally emergent categories, a structure that is, if anything, more striking for being unplanned on one side of the comparison and deliberately engineered on the other.

Prakriyā — how canonical-form training builds a psychologically real prototype

The mechanism by which sustained sāmānya drilling is expected to produce a genuine, psychologically real prototype representation, rather than merely a well-rehearsed single memorized instance indistinguishable in kind from any other memorized instance, rests on a documented feature of how prototype abstraction is thought to occur even in the emergent, unplanned case: repeated exposure to multiple instances of a category, even where those instances vary somewhat from occasion to occasion, is what drives the formation of an abstracted central-tendency representation, since the abstraction process depends on statistical averaging across instances rather than on the mere repetition of one single unvarying instance. Sāmānya training, properly conducted, is not the repetition of one perfectly identical motor sequence on every trial — no two physical repetitions of any motor sequence are ever perfectly identical, however well-drilled — but the repetition of the same intended target form across many individually slightly-varying physical executions, which is exactly the condition under which prototype-abstraction mechanisms are documented to operate most effectively, extracting the stable, intended central tendency from across the small execution-to-execution variability every individual repetition actually contains.

Udāharaṇa — a comparative case from a non-Indian gestural tradition

A useful comparative check on this account, offered here as a structural-synthetic proposal rather than as a directly documented claim within either literature, comes from considering the general design of other deliberately engineered gestural sign systems, such as the manual alphabets and formally standardized sign vocabularies developed for sign languages, which also document a canonical, dictionary-citation form for each sign — comparable in function to a hasta's sāmānya form — alongside a substantial range of context-dependent modifications made to that citation form in fluent, connected signing, modifications governed by principles of location, handshape, and movement that a fluent signer applies systematically rather than idiosyncratically. Sign-language linguistics documents this citation-versus-connected-use distinction using vocabulary considerably closer to the Nāṭyaśāstra's own sāmānya/viśeṣa pair than to prototype theory's original bird-and-furniture examples, since both the hasta system and formal sign-language lexicons are engineered rather than emergent vocabularies; the parallel is offered here as suggestive of a general design principle likely to recur across deliberately constructed gestural sign systems generally, rather than as an established historical or comparative-linguistic claim connecting the two traditions directly.

Phala — the training implication of prototype-centered organization

If the sāmānya form functions as a genuine cognitive prototype rather than merely as a conventional label for "the first thing taught," the training implication is specific and falls out directly from what is independently known about how prototype-based categories are best acquired: a category organized around a single stable central prototype is most efficiently taught by ensuring that prototype is itself well-formed and stable in the learner's memory before peripheral, less typical variants are introduced, since introducing highly variable peripheral instances before the central tendency has stabilized measurably disrupts prototype formation in the general categorization literature. This gives independent cognitive-scientific grounding, beyond the tradition's own accumulated pedagogical experience, for treating premature viśeṣa exposure as a genuine training risk rather than merely a matter of traditional preference.

05

The Neural Architecture of Category Abstraction

Neuroscientific investigation of category learning, using both functional neuroimaging in humans and single-cell electrophysiological recording in non-human primates trained on categorization tasks, has converged on a broadly consistent picture of which brain structures support the formation and use of abstracted, prototype-like category representations. The prefrontal cortex, and in particular its more anterior and lateral regions, is consistently implicated in representing category information at a relatively abstracted level, encoding the category membership of a stimulus in a form that generalizes across considerable variation in the stimulus's specific perceptual features — precisely the kind of representation prototype theory posits at the psychological level.

Working alongside prefrontal cortex, structures in the medial temporal lobe, most centrally the hippocampus, are implicated specifically in the formation of new categorical schemas from repeated exposure to varied instances, and in the consolidation of these newly formed category representations into more stable long-term memory over time and, in many studied cases, over sleep. This gives a two-stage picture of category learning at the neural level: an initial, hippocampally supported process of extracting statistical regularities from multiple varied exposures to instances of a to-be-learned category, followed by a more gradual process, implicating prefrontal cortex more heavily as learning proceeds, of representing the resulting abstracted category in a stable, generalizable form that can be applied efficiently to new instances without each new instance requiring the full, effortful comparison process the earliest exposures required.

This neural picture is directly relevant to how sāmānya training is structured. Extended, repeated drilling of a sāmānya form in relative isolation, before viśeṣa variation is introduced, is training exactly the kind of process this neural evidence associates with stable, generalizable category formation: repeated exposure to a single, consistent target form, allowing hippocampal schema-extraction and prefrontal abstraction to jointly establish a stable, generalized representation of that form before the more varied, instance-specific viśeṣa applications are introduced and begin laying down their own more particular associative memory traces on top of an already-stabilized general prototype. Introducing viśeṣa variation too early in training, before this general representation has stabilized, risks exactly the outcome the neural evidence would predict for premature exposure to high variability: a less clearly abstracted, less reliably generalizable underlying category representation, with correspondingly less reliable recognition and production of the sāmānya form itself once viśeṣa variation is layered on top of it.

Lakṣaṇa — specifying what "abstraction" means at the level of single neurons

At the level of single-cell recording, category abstraction has a specific, documented electrophysiological signature worth stating precisely rather than left as a loose metaphor: a neuron participating in an abstracted category representation is one whose firing rate reliably distinguishes category membership — responding strongly to any instance of the trained category and weakly to any instance of a contrasting category — while showing comparatively little sensitivity to the specific perceptual features that happen to distinguish one within-category instance from another. This is a stronger and more specific claim than merely finding neurons that respond to the trained stimuli at all; it is a claim about a specific pattern of generalization across within-category variation coupled with a specific pattern of discrimination across the category boundary, and it is exactly this dual pattern — high within-category generalization, sharp between-category discrimination — that recorded prefrontal neurons in primate categorization studies have been documented to display, distinguishing genuine abstracted category coding from the simpler case of neurons that merely respond to specific trained stimuli without any generalization to novel category-consistent instances at all.

Prakriyā — the documented two-stage timeline of category learning

The two-stage hippocampal-then-prefrontal timeline has a documented approximate temporal structure worth making explicit. In the earliest stage of learning a new category from varied exposure, hippocampal involvement is comparatively pronounced, consistent with the hippocampus's broader documented role in rapidly forming new associative memories from limited exposure, before those memories have had the opportunity to be gradually consolidated into more distributed, cortically stored long-term representations. As training on a given category continues and correct categorization performance stabilizes, the relative involvement of prefrontal regions increases while hippocampal involvement for that specific, now-well-learned category comparatively recedes, a shift documented in neuroimaging studies as training on a given category set proceeds across multiple sessions, and consistent with the broader neuroscientific picture of memory consolidation in which newly formed memories depend more heavily on hippocampal structures early on and become progressively less hippocampally dependent, and more dependent on distributed cortical representation, as consolidation proceeds over time and, the evidence indicates, over subsequent sleep.

Udāharaṇa — a worked training-design case

Applied concretely to sāmānya training for a single hasta, this two-stage picture suggests a specific worked prediction, offered here explicitly as a structural-synthetic proposal extrapolated from general categorization neuroscience rather than as a claim directly tested on hasta training itself: a learner in the first several sessions of drilling a new sāmānya form is, on this account, primarily engaged in the earlier, more hippocampally-mediated stage of extracting a stable target representation from the small execution-to-execution variability of repeated practice, a stage plausibly more effortful, more consciously monitored, and more error-prone than the more fluent, automatic-feeling production the same form eventually achieves once, on this account, prefrontal abstraction has taken over the bulk of the representational work. If viśeṣa variation is introduced during this still-hippocampally-dependent early window — before the account predicts the more stable, generalizable prefrontal-level representation has had the opportunity to consolidate — the new, more variable viśeṣa-related learning would be expected, on general principles of interference in memory consolidation, to compete with and potentially disrupt the still-fragile sāmānya consolidation process, providing a specific, mechanistically motivated reason to prefer a training pause of a documented consolidation-relevant duration, plausibly spanning at least one intervening sleep period, between initial sāmānya stabilization and the introduction of viśeṣa variation.

Phala — an explicit note on the limits of this evidence
